```
Michael Mattias wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:70q3lrFhk7e8U1@mid.individual.net...
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
> especially - if that next poor schmoe is myself).

Yes, I agree about being explicit, but I don't like it when "explicit" 
involves bad logic...

To take your example from another thread...

READ FILENAME
   NOT INVALID KEY
       perform Good-Read
    INVALID KEY
       perform bad-read
END-READ

This is fine because they are disparate logic paths, and the common path 
following END-READ may not relate to either of them

But...

READ FILENAME
   NOT INVALID KEY
         CONTINUE
    INVALID KEY
         perform bad-read
END-READ

..is simply redundant logic.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: To Dashwood

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-04T01:44:25+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<714n1aFinfmlU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<baa158a1-e590-485e-a8fb-1e1e7b2e461b@v13g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <4bc6a5ad-7912-4bd2-9bd2-87edb087800e@x13g2000yqf.googlegroups.com> <70q3lrFhk7e8U1@mid.individual.net> <VUSpl.17920$yr3.2416@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com> <70sjsoFhv55vU1@mid.individual.net> <31vnq4tu1p51hr9p0v6hi03vifb1pi8ck5@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sun, 1 Mar 2009 00:01:42 +1300, in comp.lang.cobol you wrote:
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> would be needed.

I spoke with people on the shop floor, because I wanted to understand the 
reasoning behind it. No-one took responsibility for it, so I suspect it is 
code that has been around for a very long time. It isn't "wrong", and I have 
seen much worse code; it just struck me as "odd"...

I'm starting to wonder if there are different accepted practices in COBOL 
code on different continents. I working over decades in multiple sites, I 
found the U.K. and New Zealand pretty much on the same page, Germany was 
similar with some minor differences, Spain was "interesting" but I never saw 
any START REVERSED or NOT INVALID KEY statements, and the USA and Italy I 
only worked on one site so it isn't a fair sample. I'd love to see what the 
Chinese are writing :-). Certainly, work being outsourced to India and Asia 
will probably have fairly rigid standards attached to it, but it would still 
be interesting to see if these diverse cultures take the same approaches.

It nearly blew my Migration Toolset out of the water and I had to burn some 
midnight oil to get it accommodated. In the end I built a stack - LIFO 
list -  (special type of collection in C# which comes with POP, PUSH, and 
PEEK methods predefined... very handy) so that any nested instances of 
(NOT) INVALID KEY/AT END  could be associated with the verbs they were 
connected with... this sort of thing...:

(Application code, being processed by a tool written in C# which translates 
COBOL ISAM access into invokes of objects in a Data Access Layer, that carry 
out the equivalent functions against a Relational DB. (The idea is to 
automate the conversion of existing code  from ISAM to RDB, but to ensure 
that exactly the same results are obtained off the new DB as were obtained 
from the ISAM files.) Obviously the new COBOL being generated must handle 
INVALID KEY/AT END conditions, even though these constructs cannot be used 
in the new environment.)


READ FILENAME
     NOT INVALID KEY
              CONTINUE
     INVALID KEY
               START KEY >= "LITERAL"
                       INVALID KEY
                               WRITE  RECORD
                                         NOT INVALIDKEY
                                                  CONTINUE
                                         INVALID KEY
                                                  SET NOT-OK TO TRUE
                                                  READ SOME-OTHER-FILE
                                                          AT END
                                                                SET 
EOF-ON-OTHER-FILE TO TRUE
                                                                CLOSE 
SOME-OTHER-FILE
                                                  END-READ
                                END-WRITE
                        NOT INVALID KEY
                                 CONTINUE
                END-START
END-READ

...using a stack ensured that I didn't pick up the "wrong" INVALID KEY 
clause for the verb being processed (Which my original code probably would 
have done...especially where the usage was inconsistent.) I redesigned and 
rewrote the whole logic for this in a day and a half then slept for about 13 
hours...:-) Fortunately, it is working very well.

Pete.
```

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-03T10:40:52-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<sdGdnXIFb7QOwDDUnZ2dnUVZ_rmdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<baa158a1-e590-485e-a8fb-1e1e7b2e461b@v13g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <4bc6a5ad-7912-4bd2-9bd2-87edb087800e@x13g2000yqf.googlegroups.com> <70q3lrFhk7e8U1@mid.individual.net> <VUSpl.17920$yr3.2416@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com> <70sjsoFhv55vU1@mid.individual.net> <31vnq4tu1p51hr9p0v6hi03vifb1pi8ck5@4ax.com> <714n1aFinfmlU1@mid.individual.net> <75jqq4luior77ngkpqlipvf132dhrthe09@4ax.com>`

```
Your sig:

"In no part of the constitution is more wisdom to be found,
than in the clause which confides the question of war or peace
to the legislature, and not to the executive department."

- James Madison

Needs a certain interpretation. The "wisdom" to which Madison refers is the 
warm, fuzzy feeling that the Congress gets when they believe they actually 
matter. While Congress has the sole authority to DECLARE war, the president 
has the unfettered ability to WAGE war. For example, Bill Clinton waged war 
against more countries than any other since FDR (Iraq, Afghanistan, Bosnia, 
Serbia, Haiti, Albania, Somalia, and Sudan).

Congress CAN cut off funding for the war, but that has its difficulties. 
When Teddy Roosevelt wanted to send the American fleet on an 
around-the-world tout, Congress balked at providing the funds. Roosevelt 
replied: "Well, I have enough money to send them HALF way around the world. 
Let's see if Congress wants them back." A more recent example is that 
Congress didn't cut off funds for Viet Nam until a year after the last U.S. 
troops left the country.
```

#### ↳ Re: (OT) In search of a definition

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-02T14:44:29+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<710rvvFijitgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<Kk5ql.1469$Za7.1217@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> As someone who "grew up" in an IBM mainframe environment and now
> primarily works under Windows XP, I am in search of a "good
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Download the libgmp package ...

I first encountered this in Java, where Rogers Cadenhead defines it as "a 
group of related classes that serve a common purpose".

Java is built almost entirely around "packages" which contain all the 
classes to do a specific kind of activity. For example, one form of 
graphical interface in Java uses the Graphics class. But this class exists 
inside a "package" of other related classes called the "Abstract Window 
Toolkit". So, if you need to have a graphic display in your Java program you 
would start by importing the class you need from the package:

import java.awt.Graphics

This is fine for simple graphics but if you want something much sexier you 
would probably use Java Foundation Classes and these are "packaged" into the 
java.awt.swing package.

import java.awt.swing

"Swing" as it is commonly referred to, has a number of advantages over the 
standard graphics package, but it is HUGE...(in Java terms). It has 8 
subordinate packages, more than 20 interfaces and over 75 classes. (I like 
Swing because it is comprised entirely of components...) Still, I guess size 
is relative; the latest incarnation of the .NET framework has around 70,000 
classes...it is no wonder that the download is .6 GB compressed.

So, you can see that a "package" IS a convenient way of grouping related 
classes together. Very often, the "package" is implemented as a dynamic link 
library (not usually in Java, but there are other systems that use 
packages).  The difference between a .DLL and a package is more of a 
conceptual one. (A package could contain several .DLLs)


>
> It is "sort-of" an application - but the same "application" can have a
> "development" and a "binary" package.  There also seem to be cases
> where it takes "multiple packages" to get something to actually work.

Yes, you might have source code or run time code both "packaged" separately 
or together. Java implements this through "jars" (Java archive files) as a 
convenient means of transporting packages (and beans).

>
> This is for my "getting started" document for OpenCOBOL - for Windows
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Any suggestions?


As the term is primarily related to Object Oriented Programming, people 
without this background may have difficulty in visualizing it. However, as 
the examples above show, the concept is not that difficult. After using 
packages and dealing with them for some time the concept becomes established 
and it is really more than Rogers' simple definition above, although that is 
an excellent start point.

"a group of related classes that serve a common purpose".  is not helpful if 
you don't really understand what a "class" is.

<Warning! This is a brain dump from Pete. It involves ideas and concepts 
which can lead to insanity in people who are already unstable. If you are 
taking tranquilizers, skip this section.>

Taking a leaf out of Robert Persig's book ("Zen and the art of motorcycle 
maintenance"), think of it hierarchically, like this:

Package
   Class... (there can be many within a Package)
        Method (there can be many within a Class)
              Function (there can be many within a Method)

As long as you are happy with the understanding of a Function (most 
programmers are...) then this should make sense, even without an OO 
background.

The conceptual bit concerns the implicit relationships between the above 
elements.

A Method defines behaviour and can have disparate unrelated functions in it 
(although often the functions in a method ARE related. Certainly, they are 
all part of achieving the goals of the method. For example, a method might 
contain functions to calculate a square root and to find the length of a 
string . Not obviously related when viewed separately... :-) Put the 
"Method" umbrella over them and suddenly it becomes clear what they are 
trying to achieve.

The Class establishes a higher level set of goals and acts as an "umbrella" 
for the methods, in very much the same way as the Method acted for the 
functions. (The Methods actually define and implement "behaviours" of the 
Class.)

(At this point some of you are thinking "Methods are just procedures; I know 
all about that" and that is where it all goes pear shaped. You are applying 
ITSA when you should be applying ITSLIKE (if you REALLY must apply 
anything... sometimes it is best to cear your mind and pretend you've not 
seen anything before. This is why Newbies pick up OO better than old 
hands... It is the CONCEPTS that make all the difference, although that is 
not immediately obvious when you come to it first up. Most of us try to 
apply and relate things we already know to things we are trying to learn. 
There are times when it is best NOT to do that.)

So, we have a Class that behaves in certain ways which we control. And we 
understand that its behaviours are designed to achieve the goals of the 
Class.

Now, we take an even higher level goal and apply a group of Classes to it. 
They are related in the same way that the methods relate to the Class, and 
the functions relate to each method:  That's a PACKAGE.

Here's a contrived sample:

Package Goal:            Get dressed.
    Class  Goal:            Put on Day wear
         (set global "Time" property to "day")
          Method Goal:    Underwear is on.            Behaviour: Put on 
underwear
                  Function:   Selection of underpants
                  Function:   Don underpants
                  Function:   Selection of socks
                  Function:   Don Socks
                  Function:   Selection of bra  (depends on "sex" property 
of the Method.)
                  Function:   Don bra
                  Function:   Selection of girdle/corset/garter 
belt/suspenders  (depends on "sex" property of the Method.)
                  Function:   Don girdle/corset/garter belt/suspenders
                  Function:   Selection of stockings  (depends on "sex" 
property of the Method.)
                  Function:   Don stockings
         Method Goal:    Outerwear is on              Behaviour:  Put on Day 
outerwear
                  Function:   Selection of trousers
                  Function:   Don trousers
                  Function:   Selection of skirt/dress (depends on "sex" 
property of the Method.)
                  Function:   Don skirt/dress
                  Function:   Selection of shirt/blouse (depends on "sex" 
property of the Method.)
                  Function:   Don shirt/blouse
          Method Goal:    Shoes are on                  Behaviour:  Put on 
shoes
                  Function:   Selection of shoes  (depends on "sex" property 
of the Method and global "Time" property)
                  Function:   Don shoes
    Class  Goal:            Put on Evening wear
          (set global "Time" property to "evening")
          Method Goal:    Underwear is on.            Behaviour: Put on 
underwear
                  (Reuse Method from Put on Day wear)
          Method Goal:    Outerwear is on              Behaviour:  Put on 
Evening outerwear
                  (Reuse Method from Put on Day wear)
          Method Goal:    Shoes are on                  Behaviour:  Put on 
shoes
                  (Reuse Method from Put on Day wear)
    Class  Goal:            Put on special clothing
                  (set global property "Type" to "beach", "scuba", "ski" 
etc...)
          Method Goal:    Underwear is on.            Behaviour: Put on 
underwear
                  (Reuse Method from Put on Day wear; only invoked if 
required)
          Method Goal:    Outerwear is on              Behaviour:  Put on 
special outerwear
                  Function:   Selection of special outerwear
                  Function:   Don special outerwear
          Method Goal:    Shoes are on                  Behaviour:  Put on 
special shoes
                  Function:   Selection of special shoes  (check "sex" 
property of the Method where required)
                  Function:   Don shoes

You can see that, using the above package it is possible to dress for most 
occasions.

The package can be designed in a number of ways for physical implementation. 
It might be feasible to use overrides and extensions of common classes, in 
order to modify behaviour for special cases, for example. But the important 
point here is the CONCEPT.

</Warning! This is a brain dump from Pete. It involves ideas and concepts 
which can lead to insanity in people who are already unstable. If you are 
taking tranquilizers, skip this section.>

Having had a shot at putting the requisite concepts in place, I would offer 
the following as a definition of a PACKAGE:

"A package implicitly defines, and explicitly implements, the highest level 
goals to be achieved by a given process. It is an umbrella for the 
sub-processes (Classes) contained within it and provides a convenient means 
of transporting them across platforms."

Pete.
```

Pete,
  I found your information on "packages" interesting, BUT ...

I don't think that exactly matches the type of "package" that I am talking 
about.  What I was looking for (and eventually found) was the definition in 
Wikipedia at:

      http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_package_(installation)

and the spcific part that I used in my document was,

"A software package refers to computer software packaged in an archive format to 
be installed by a package management system or a self-sufficient installer.

Linux distributions are normally segmented into packages. Each package contains 
a specific application or service. Examples of packages include a library for 
handling the PNG image format, a collection of fonts, or a web browser."

Although I can imagine this being related to the OO concept (and I think that I 
actually first heard THAT meaning related to ADA),  I do think this meaning is 
an "installation packaging" issue and not a software design/implementation 
issue.

```
On Tue, 3 Mar 2009 11:16:17 -0800 (PST), riplin@Azonic.co.nz wrote:

>The concept of a package is not related to _any_ language. It happens
>that you never met the term before using Java and thus think that it
>is related to that language. Basically Java used the keyword 'package'
>where C used #import (and it does mean something somewhat more,
>granted), but 'package' is used by C

One difficulty with using English language words for technical terms,
as found in programming, is that words carry baggage.   

Just as there is misunderstanding about evolution being "only a
theory", because of multiple understandings on what "theory" means, we
have words meaning different things within the discipline of
programming.

Over the years "database" has and still means different things to
different people.   "Package" means specific different things to, say
Java programmers and Oracle DBAs.   It is easy to assume we are
speaking the same language when the words are the same, and the
dialects are close.   

But assuming doesn't make for the most complete communication, and can
often miss important or even vital parts of our process.

Even if we used made up words (or if we didn't know English),
technical terms used in our business would still quickly acquire
baggage that could mislead us.
```

This is often due to laziness. Some may use 'database' unqualified
when they are referring to a specific subset or product, such as
RDBMS.

A 'database' can be any organised set of data. A related collection of
ISAM files is as much a database as any other, it happens that these
may not have a DBMS, or they may be managed manually.

> "Package" means specific different things to, say
> Java programmers and Oracle DBAs.

In what way do you think that these two groups use _different_
meanings ?

"""This chapter shows you how to bundle related PL/SQL programming
resources into a package. The resources might include a collection of
procedures or a pool of type definitions and variable declarations.
For example, a Human Resources package might contain hiring and firing
procedures. Once written, your general-purpose package is compiled,
then stored in an Oracle database, where its contents can be shared by
many applications."""

What would a Java programmer find different to how he uses the term ?

What is a class but "a collection of procedures or a pool of type
definitions and variable declarations" ?

The point about 'package', whether it be in C, Java, or PL/SQL is that
it is exactly 'a bundle' and that "its contents can be shared by many
applications".


>  It is easy to assume we are
> speaking the same language when the words are the same, and the
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

What actual point are you attempting to make ?

The SF author Federik Pohl wrote an autobiography named _The Way The

expert on Tiberius.   One time he was with some other scholars with
his interest and they had a disagreement on a particular issue.

supported him.   The other guy accepted it - not noticing that the
initials at the end of the article were FP.  (I looked up that article
in my Britannica when I read this).

- **References:** `<Kk5ql.1469$Za7.1217@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <710rvvFijitgU1@mid.individual.net> <34hmq45h5ttltacte997g44ohulenie0o9@4ax.com> <711is2FigunbU1@mid.individual.net> <84752991-7061-4422-9ce8-512fae8a5740@v18g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <716440Fjju1hU1@mid.individual.net> <k7itq4h6mv4385i6eqtrfpijlbfkk19f6g@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> To win his argument, Fred pulled out an Encyclopedia Britannica which
> supported him.   The other guy accepted it - not noticing that the
> initials at the end of the article were FP.  (I looked up that article
> in my Britannica when I read this).

A fine rhetorical move.

Of course, there are multiple authorities at work here. The Britannica
has a certain amount of ethetic standing (ie, a perception of
authority) for many audiences, including apparently the person Pohl
was arguing with. And part of that is a reputation for vetting authors
and articles. So in a case like this, Pohl gains some transitive ethos
- the fact that he was published by Britannica is an implied endorsement.

But if we take the anecdote's account as accurate (and I don't see any
reason not to), then the other scholar just relied on the ethos of
Britannica itself in making his decision.
```

nor why a shunned behavior is deemed to be wrong.    This can be a
survival characteristic, as long as we don't go to war with the other
guys for our Righteous causes.    (a kid knows Mom will be mad if he
climbs on the hot stove).

>No wonder folks who see things 'otherwise' have such a difficult time... 
>they are going against rules for survival ingrained sometime around the 
>time of Leakey's Lucy.

I believe we all have conditions where we see things otherwise, and
that we all have conditions where we prefer agreement.   And the
amount of agreement that we are comfortable with varies.   It's not
even close to two-valued.

My tendency is to strongly doubt it when someone claims to be
completely "otherwise" in this.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: (OT) In search of a definition

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-03T11:15:51-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gojme102qg3@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<710rvvFijitgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<Kk5ql.1469$Za7.1217@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <710rvvFijitgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> William M. Klein wrote:
>> As someone who "grew up" in an IBM mainframe environment and now
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> As the term is primarily related to Object Oriented Programming...

Not in this context, where it has long been used for software bundles
(sources, binaries, or both) regardless of the source language or
programming paradigm.

The term "package" to refer to an installable software collection has
been used for decades. See, for example, this 1961 DEC announcement:

http://www.textfiles.com/bitsavers/pdf/dec/pdp1/memos/M-1112_BinPunLd_Jun61.pdf

which refers to "loading" a "package" that provides a memory load/dump
utility.

In the Linux / Unix arena, this usage was popularized by various
software package installation managers, such as Red Hat's rpm (Red Hat
Package Manager).
```

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gom98016r0@news7.newsguy.com>`

- **References:** `<Kk5ql.1469$Za7.1217@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <710rvvFijitgU1@mid.individual.net> <gojme102qg3@news6.newsguy.com> <3ebbec49-292e-419d-be8c-704fb72e0e52@e1g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz wrote:
> On Mar 4, 5:15 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Or well before that by pkgadd, pkginfo, etc.

Yes, those are other members of that set.

I used rpm as an example because it's well-known, and it's a Linux
package manager, which is what we had originally been discussing.
(Bill asked about translating the "software package" concept from
Linux to Windows.)

RPM appeared circa 1995. SysV package management (pkgadd, etc) came
with, what, SVR4 in 1990? I suppose it might have been part of SVR3 in
1986, but I don't remember it in SVR3. At any rate, by the time it
appeared, the "package" usage in question was well-established.

And the additional features introduced by the advanced Linux package
managers (rpm, apt, etc), such as built-in network clients and more
powerful dependency handling, did much to popularize them. SysV
package handling was certainly a huge step forward from the Wild West
days of tar+compress, shar, archie, etc; but the newer managers make
things much easier for the administrator. That's not an unqualified
good, but it has brought package management to non-specialist Linux users.
```

<a id="t-ad24a783c6"></a>
## Thread: ISPF Edit/View HILITE to HTML macros - update — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`Web, XML, modern integration`](topics.md#web)_

### ISPF Edit/View HILITE to HTML macros - update

- **From:** "Robert AH Prins" <spamtrap@prino.org>
- **Date:** 2009-03-01T08:50:08
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.pl1,comp.lang.rexx,comp.lang.cobol,bit.listserv.tsorexx,bit.listserv.ispf-l,bit.listserv.ibm-main
- **Message-ID:** `<70ut1eFhqtgeU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Hi all,

Around 18 months ago I made a post about the availability of a set of 
REXX edit macros/execs that can be used to convert PL/I, REXX, 
assembler, JCL, COBOL and, limited to what I needed, SuperC output) into 
a format that is for all intents and purposes the same as when the 
member is looked at in ISPF's Edit or View using HI RESET + HI PAREN - 
there are a few small differences, mainly because I (hopefully) 
corrected parsing JCL in-stream data, no-operand assembler 
opcodes/directives and removed the SuperC HILITE bug where the last two 
columns are displayed in green. :)

The set of (currently) nine macros consists of:

EHIASM   - convert assembler to Edit HILITE'd HTML
EHICOBOL - convert COBOL to Edit HILITE'd HTML
EHIHELP  - general help 'screen'
EHIJCL   - convert JCL to Edit HILITE'd HTML
EHINONE  - convert any text to Edit HTML (no HILITE)
EHIPLI   - convert PL/I to Edit HILITE'd HTML (upgraded to Enterprise 
PL/I V3.7 keywords)
EHIREXX  - convert REXX to Edit HILITE'd HTML
EHISUPC  - convert SuperC to Edit HILITE'd HTML (still limited to what I 
require...)
EHISUPP  - general support (produce ISPF popup panel to set options and 
generate the actual HTML)

BTW, besides working as edit macro under ISPF (and as a TSO command) on 
z/OS, they also work on Regina.

They are available on the cbttape.org site 
http://www.cbttape.org/ftp/updates/CBT769.zip, but sadly the versions in 
that zipped XMIT file no longer work in FireFox 3 (and the fancy output 
never worked on IE6). The original developer of the Javascript that is 
responsible for the fancy ISPF like screen has given me an update and it 
works. If I can manage to get my code back onto the system at my new 
workplace, I'll produce an update in the next few days, but if you 
desperately needed it yesterday, drop me a line, (email 
x2c(88899389A3857C97998995964B9585A3) (EBCDIC), put '[HILITE]' somewhere 
in the subject AND DO NOT SEND EMAIL IN HTML FORMAT!

Indicate if you want the code as nine(+1) text files or as a PDS in 
zipped XMIT format and make sure that any return message with '[HILITE]' 
in the subject isn't stopped (or worse, returned to me) by any spam 
filters.

Robert
```

>
> DECLARATIVES are useful, but having them always at the start of the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I hope the above simple syntax (Microsoft way) will do the thing. In
Cobol I usually use this code;

           *>
           *>Set up the exception handling
             INVOKE entrycallback "new" USING z"onExceptionERR"
                                    RETURNING osException

             INVOKE exceptmgr "register" USING oleExceptMgr
                                               osException

                        :
                        :
                        :
                        :


       999-Callback section.
       entry "onExceptionERR" using by reference lnkErrorObject
                                    by reference lnkErrorNumber
                                    by reference lnkErrorText.

                        :
                        :
                        :
                        :

           exit program.

           stop run.

It's quite long coding but you could modify it according to taste, but
then again "every error" will be trapped on this net and that's not
advisable.
```

On 27 Feb, 22:05, "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbr...@efirstbank.com>

> Interesting that Pete mentioned having seen use of INVALID KEY and NOT
> INVALID KEY recently.  I was pondering the use of this just yesterday.  We
…[95 more quoted lines elided]…
> Lakewood, CO  USA

AT END, INVALID KEY, etc were introduced with the early COBOL
standards/definitions, File status was a later addition.  I think that
these days it is better to check for file status, but sometimes
programs read better for using AT END and INVALID KEY as well.

Robert

<a id="t-e2630ec11d"></a>
## Thread: (OT) In search of a definition — 1 msgs

### Re: (OT) In search of a definition

- **From:** David Essex <nospam@nospam.nospam>
- **Date:** 2009-03-01T22:52:31-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a6d19$49ab585e$cf701e34$20386@PRIMUS.CA>`

```
In order to make OpenCOBOL more portable, the original developers choose
to generate C code and UN*X as their development platform.

In theory any C compiler can be used to create a binary, as long as the
run-time support libraries are available for that C compiler.

The only officially supported Wind*ws port requires the Cygw*n POSIX
UN*X like emulation layer.
Unofficially there are at least two native Wind*ws ports available.
One uses MinGW (Minimalist GNU For Wind*ws) and the other uses MSVC.

Enclosed some links[1] to both ports.
These options are strictly command line.

1) OpenCobol Wind*ws native ports
OpenCobol 1.1 VS2005 binaries
http://www.kiska.net/opencobol/

http://opencobol.no-ip.org/download.html

http://www.opencobol.org/modules/newbb/viewtopic.php?topic_id=487&forum=1

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ [OT] Pack-Ratting (was Re: COBOL program on punched cards)

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-03-03T14:09:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gojdn6$lv6$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<ff014cbd-7fb6-4a9f-a388-51178d59f76e@u18g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <goi8o6$4h1$1@news.motzarella.org> <714mrhFj8pbvU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <714mrhFj8pbvU1@mid.individual.net>,
Bill Gunshannon <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote:

[snip]

>I just picked up a deck of punched COBOL
>while cleaning up my attic yesterday but having kept and preserved
>these for over 30 years I am unlikely to let them out of my control.

No offense intended to you, Mr Gunshannon, or to anyone else - even 
myself, not that such a thing might ever happen, mind you - but this is 
something I have noticed and has caused me to muse about self-fulfilling 
criteria.

A person has a physical object, call it X... and there comes a time when X 
no longer serves an immediate need.  Relying on experience ('that which is 
not needed now has been later') this object gets set aside.

Time passes... sometimes *much* time passes.  For whatever reason this 
same person then stumbles across the X, which has not served a use for a 
goodly while.

What is it that goes on when the person says 'Well, I haven't used this in 
a goodly long while... and there's even less of a possibility that I'll 
have need of it in the future... but since I've saved it so very long it 
must be Very Important, I can't throw it out now!'?

(This happened to me a few years back when I ran across a still-in-the-box 
copy of some manner of 'all in one' software suite... manuals, 5.25" 
floppy disks, never used.  I thought 'this is of recent Oldene Dayse, it 
is of interest, it... it is a piece of obsolete software for an obsolete 
operating system and I ain't no museum, away with you!')

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Pack-Ratting (was Re: COBOL program on punched cards)

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-03-03T15:16:23+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gojhk7$1f5$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<ff014cbd-7fb6-4a9f-a388-51178d59f76e@u18g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <714mrhFj8pbvU1@mid.individual.net> <gojdn6$lv6$1@reader1.panix.com> <714tisFisu4aU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <714tisFisu4aU1@mid.individual.net>,
Bill Gunshannon <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote:
>In article <gojdn6$lv6$1@reader1.panix.com>,
>	docdwarf@panix.com () writes:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>need all the time it has been up there?

Assumption of fact not in evidence, Mr Gunshannon; your description was 
'having kept and preserved these for over 30 years', not 'having kept and 
preserved and used (etc)'.  Based on the description given there was no 
use, no need for a deck of punched cards for three decades; the 
description still holds.

[snip]

>> For whatever reason this 
>> same person then stumbles across the X, which has not served a use for a 
>> goodly while.
>
>Once again. that is your opinion.

Once again, that was your description.  When there is a use for something 
it gets put in a place where less energy is expended in establishing its 
location so that the use might enacted; to leave it in a place where it 
only gets 'picked up while cleaning' generates inefficiency.

>
>> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>where did my wife put those divorce papers she wanted me to sign?

In a place where she can find them, one hopes... she wants or forsees the 
need for them, she puts them where they can be most efficiently found.  
>
>> 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>what you started this missive with, insulting.

Sensitive, aren't we?  Read carefully, Mr Gunshannon; the original 
setting-aside was based on reason ('I have had use for such things before, 
it might be useful to have them around later')... but there seems to be no 
statute of limitations, no 'I do not believe that I have reason to 
conclude that I will need X after now'.

>My attic is full of
>things (like my card decks) that are of sentimental value to me and my
>family.  And I will keep them.

*Bingo*.  'Sentimental value' indicates emotional attachment and emotions, 
by definition, are not rational; what is being dealt with here, then, 
seems to be a product of emotional behavior, not rational.  It seems less 
'I will have reason to use X' and more 'I have positive sentiments towards 
X'.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Pack-Ratting (was Re: COBOL program on punched cards)

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2009-03-03T11:12:23-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5bsqq4dndjouvnj089p4qka9pd2ou8eh3n@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<ff014cbd-7fb6-4a9f-a388-51178d59f76e@u18g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <714mrhFj8pbvU1@mid.individual.net> <gojdn6$lv6$1@reader1.panix.com> <714tisFisu4aU1@mid.individual.net> <gojhk7$1f5$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
Sometimes I compare the process of getting a new IS system to moving
to a new house.   It's the only time when many people go through their
garage or basement to decide what to keep and what to get rid of.

Starting fresh means making an analysis of our needs against the costs
of keeping stuff that isn't needed anymore.

At home, we often also go through levels of downsizing.    From the
big house with bedrooms for the children, to the smaller condo that
still has a basement for my books, to the retirement home, and then
the nursing home.    Each level changes the cost/benefit analysis of
keeping stuff.
```

know ...".  Sentiment or extreme pragmatism?

>The moral is, though, that there are times you really don't know.  I have a
>toolcrib app which I haven't used since about the same time but "you never
>know ...".  Sentiment or extreme pragmatism?

I'd say 'neither'.  E'en a fool in the wrong can find a cite of Scripture 
to support an argument and it has been taught what does *not* a summer 
make... but 'you never know' (usually crooned 'you ne-ver 
knoooo-oooowww!') is what I call The Appeal to Universal Ignorance.

No, I cannot say that one will never need source-code stored on 
punch-cards in an attic, for a system long-since decommissioned for a 
company which no longer exists and themselves untouched for decades... but 
proving a logical negative can be a dicey matter, to say the least.  

For a great part, though, it seems that Mr Gunshannon is, in part, 
correct... that what is dealt with here is a matter of emotion, not 
reason.

DD

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:gojo93$1r4$1@reader1.panix.com...
> No, I cannot say that one will never need source-code stored on
> punch-cards in an attic, for a system long-since decommissioned for a
> company which no longer exists and themselves untouched for decades... but
> proving a logical negative can be a dicey matter, to say the least.
>


 I often hear that argument, usually when it comes to talking about
sasquatches, but in far more mundane discussions also.  I refer to the form
"it's impossible to prove that xxx doesn't exist".  That may be true, but if
you can prove that xxx CANNOT exist then you've also proved that it DOES NOT
exist.

Having said that, I concede that it's exceedingly unlikely that any ideas
preserved in a deck of cards in the attic will be important - for one thing
the deck is almost certainly unreadable, and for another I dare say that
there is no such thing as an idea converted into a programming language that
has been done so exactly ONCE.  I can think of one of my clever functions
that I haven't seen duplicated anywhere, but that just means I haven't
looked everywhere!

pl
```

There is significant disagreement about what CANNOT exist.   Even when
we disregard the supernatural, there some things in nature that boggle
the mind.    The concept in quantum physics of simultaneous behavior
of particles at a distance is one that many (most?) physics assume is
just observed, that when we learn more, we will discover it isn't
really so.

Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Tue, 3 Mar 2009 11:58:19 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> really so.

I'm assuming you're talking about entanglement and EPR?

I'm one who believes it is exactly what it appears to be, even though it 
would seem logically inconsistent in the reference frame we normally apply.

What encourages me to this position is that if everything emerged from a 
single event which created matter, energy, space, and time, then it would be 
surprising if things were NOT connected in some way which is not immediately 
obvious (possibly through the other dimensions which are posited to be 
necessary), because at one point everything WAS just one "something". Hard 
to describe in words, and treads the boundaries of Physics and MetaPhysics 
which is always risky ground...nevertheless, I don't find the reported 
behaviours to be unacceptable or down to our inability to measure properly, 
or simply apparencies due to lack of understanding. To me it seems fair and 
reasonable... :-)

Pete.
```

Books are very odd things... on the one hand I hold some of them nigh 
sacred and on the other hand I realise that publishing companies are 
industries like any others, they have to keep churning out... stuff.  I 
know, for instance, that the science of organic chemistry has changed 
quite a bit since I first laid hands on my copy of Morrison and Boyd's 
tome on the subject (not many folks speak of 'carbonium ions' any more) 
and yet there it sits, on my shelf.

I once heard an aphorism, 'Many scholars are like donkeys, all they do is 
carry loads of books'... and it came to me that one can carry both the 
physical object or the memory of it.  To have is one thing, to be able to 
use for benefit or pleasure is quite another... and I try to avoid the 
utilitarian sense of 'use'; the use of some books I've read has been to 
give me what I call 'pretty thoughts' - I've not found much utility in the 
concept of a sphere, a closed figure, whose radii are all parallel to each 
other and meet at an infinitely great distance from the surface which is 
at the center (Lobachevski's plagiarised 'hypersphere') - but the thought 
of such a thing causes me to smile.

'When I get a little money I buy books; and if there is any left I buy 
food and clothes.' - attributed to Erasmus of Rotterdam

DD

<a id="t-ac8ee1e773"></a>
## Thread: Never seen before syntax — 2 msgs

### Never seen before syntax

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-03T17:48:39-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Qkjrl.812$BR.581@en-nntp-04.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Just as a follow-up on another thread

1) Having NOT INVALID KEY coded *before* an INVALID KEY was illegal in the '85 
Standard.  The '02 Standard lets them go in either order

2) START REVERSED was non-conforming in the '85 Standard.  In fact, IBM *still* 
doesn't support "reversed" READ or START.

3) All the "NO" phrases were added in the '85 Standard.  Therefore, if working 
with "old" COBOL code (originally developed for the '74 - or even '68 
Standards), you won't see these constructs (or CONTINUE).
```

#### ↳ Re: Never seen before syntax

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-04T13:51:05+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7161jrFj7c1pU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<Qkjrl.812$BR.581@en-nntp-04.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> Just as a follow-up on another thread
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> or even '68 Standards), you won't see these constructs (or CONTINUE).

Thanks Bill.

As usual, you are a font of knowledge on these thngs :-)

Pete.
```

<a id="t-b24f1a8adc"></a>
## Thread: as the case may be — 33 msgs

### as the case may be

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-05T16:11:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
ISPF in z/OS, or at least how we have it set up, automatically does 'CAPS
ON' if you choose to edit a member that has no lower case in it.  On the
other hand it does 'CAPS OFF' if it does have lower case.  This causes data
entered in lower case in these programs to remain in lower case, where the
programmers here are used to it being automatically converted to upper
case.

Number one, is there a way to turn this 'feature' off?

Number two, should we turn it off?

As we migrate from VSE to z/OS I am almost thinking that we should convert
our ALL CAPS source code to all lower case.  Well, except for data within
quotes.  I find all lowercase much more pleasant to look at.

But not everyone agrees.  What do other shops do?

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: as the case may be

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-05T17:43:00-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Z9qdnfHbCqYc_i3UnZ2dnUVZ_vGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```

#### ↳ Re: as the case may be

- **From:** Joel C Ewing <jREMOVEcCAPSewing@acm.org>
- **Date:** 2009-03-14T13:46:08-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<SXSul.120971$fM1.68482@newsfe14.iad>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
If one is going to insist on mono-case, for a given font size all caps 
is much easier for my old eyes to read than all lower case.  I don't 
perceive text that is all "whispering" any more aesthetically pleasing 
than text that is all "shouting".

Whether you do mono case or mixed case should depend on the context. 
For contexts where conventional use of capitalization is expected 
(documentation, or other text intended to be read by humans) there is 
usually no excuse these days for not using correct mixed case 
conventions.  Even with programming languages that are for the most part 
case-insensitive, installation conventions can choose to use selective 
capitalization to make it easier to visually distinguish constants, 
variables, keywords, etc.; and I am convinced that consistent use of 
such visual assists makes it easier for others to follow the code.  I 
would very much discourage a one-solution-fits-all approach.

ISPF provides a number of mechanisms for encouraging edit conventions. 
The principal mechanism is the edit profile, based on the Low-Level 
Qualifier of the dataset name (and whether the DS is FB or VB format). 
It greatly simplifies things in ISPF if you use a consistent and limited 
number of LLQ names for datasets which should have the same editing 
conventions.  For things that can't be done easily with the profiles 
alone, we also have a REXX Site Edit Macro, which is invoked with each 
edit initiation and which can do such things as use the DSN LLQ to 
determine if it would be appropriate to issue edit commands under the 
covers to override user edit profile values or other editor assumed 
defaults.

By the combination of setting up some standard "locked" installation 
default edit profiles and setting up a site macro, it is possible to 
steer users to the desired conventions; but users can still deliberately 
override those conventions if it conflicts with their requirements.

Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> ISPF in z/OS, or at least how we have it set up, automatically does 'CAPS
> ON' if you choose to edit a member that has no lower case in it.  On the
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

Robert Jones wrote:

…[49 more quoted lines elided]…

Programmers, of course, a breed unto ourselves! :)

Stuff that could potentially be read by end-user humans must adhere to 
higher aesthetic and clarity standards than that which will only be 
viewed by gurus with enhanced interpretive skills.  :)

In an ideal world I would like to see more programming conventions to 
use mixed case in meaningful and consistent ways, even in COBOL; but 
when we have 10,000+ existing COBOL programs plus additional copybooks 
in mono case, who is going to foot the bill for case-enhancing existing 
code when there is no syntactic or functional requirement to force it? 
There goes consistency, which would unfortunately make things more 
confusing rather than less if we tried to switch at this stage in the 
game.
```

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gpm44702qqa@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gpltf3$ejb$2@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <SXSul.120971$fM1.68482@newsfe14.iad> <7eb01b59-21f8-41cc-adf2-13b0d7527519@o11g2000yql.googlegroups.com> <%TUul.50873$Tp5.19525@newsfe13.iad> <gpltf3$ejb$2@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <%TUul.50873$Tp5.19525@newsfe13.iad>,
> Joel C Ewing  <jREMOVEcCAPSewing@acm.org> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> to code JCL in mixed case.

Indeed. Programmers often forget about the human audience for their
code, but sometimes they forget about the machine audience.

Just last Wednesday I gave a presentation on the readability of code,
and treating coding as technical writing, at the annual conference of
the Association of Teachers of Technical Writing. My focus was
primarily on mixing information for those two audiences (human and
machine), though, and I didn't have time to take up visual-design
issues like letter case.

(If anyone's curious, you can see a PDF of the slides and my speaker
notes at http://ideoplast.org/code/index.html.)

If I had to guess, I'd hypothesize that more practitioners would show
best comprehension (fewer errors, faster reading time, or both) with
mixed case - because then case can serve as an additional channel. I'd
expect lower case to outperform upper case, because there's more
variety of letter form in lower case (thanks mostly to ascenders and
descenders), especially when using monospace fonts. More-varied letter
forms make it easier for the eye to track within the line of text.

But I don't know offhand of any empirical studies that have tested
that. There probably are some, but I don't have 'em handy.
```

I'll note only that empirical studies of readability often produce
results that contradict readers' common-sense assumptions about their
own reading practices. In short, what you expect may well not be the
case, and what you prefer may not be optimal.

Of course, that doesn't mean your preference is "wrong" - just that
you may prefer something that's not optimal for reading speed and/or
accuracy. We choose to compromise reading speed, ease, and accuracy
all the time for a variety of reasons.

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-03-20T13:00:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gq041i$7io$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com> <gptu3t$ljv$2@reader1.panix.com> <72fl5gFpfvsqU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <72fl5gFpfvsqU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>> In article <gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com>,
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>My notebook is much better looking than I am... :-)

Not having seen either I cannot generate an informed conclusion, 
concurring or disagreeing... but I was doing a bit of musing regarding the 
difference between 'Overman' and 'Superman' as translations of 
'Uebermensch' that you might find interesting.

Both have, as their base, a derivation from the same Ancient Greek word 
'huper'.  Since Ancient Greek did not have a letter representing the 'h' 
sound it was indicated by an accent-mark indicating they are to be 
'aspirated' (exhaled, 'huuuhhhh'd before pronouncing); the word is 
rendered as (aspirated-upsilon)-pi-episilon-rho and pronounced (as is 
taught nowadays) as 'huper' (HOO-pehr).

Upsilon has two forms, upper- and lower-case; uppercase looks like a 
modern 'Y' and lowercase like a 'u'.  A consonant-shift in the aspiration 
and use of the uppercase form brought this word into Latin as 'super'; 
retaining the aspiration and using the uppercase form is found, still, in 
the modifying prefix of 'hyper'... but that's a bit of trivia.

'Huper' also worked its way northward, dropping its aspiration but gaining 
a consonant-shift, into the German 'ueber'... which is the predecessor of 
the Old English 'ofer', whence is derived 'over'.

So... hyper, super, ueber, over... all from the same, yet all different.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: as the case may be

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-21T10:02:18+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<72ieisFq73e2U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com> <gptu3t$ljv$2@reader1.panix.com> <72fl5gFpfvsqU1@mid.individual.net> <gq041i$7io$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <72fl5gFpfvsqU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD
Fascinating. Thanks for that.

I think that, for me, anyway, I try not to be too literal when translating 
between languages.  Tools like Babelfish are really excellent insofar as 
they allow us to at least get insight into what someone is trying to say in 
a language we are not familiar with. But they don't convey the true nuances 
and rich background of the meaning. Language, as an expression of thought, 
has subtleties that are cultural and sometimes regional. Mechanical 
translation rarely picks that up.

Sometimes when you learn a new language it has words which translate to 
something in your own language, but after using the new language for a while 
you realise that the literal translation is not adequate, and it doesn't 
mean what you thought it did.

(The Sicilian villain in "The Princess Bride" keeps saying 
"Inconceivable".Finally, Inigo, the Spaniard says to him:"You keep using 
that word. I do no think it means what you think it means...") [BTW, if any 
of you haven't seen this try and rent it on video... It is a beautifully 
written and delightful story that people of all ages can enjoy and 
appreciate at different levels. The music is by Mark Knopfler of "Dire 
Straits" and it has some hauntng melodies in it.]

An example from German could be "schadenfreude". "schaden" to damage, 
"freude" joy  so, literally:" Joy at damage" A close English approximation 
could be "glee" or "gleeful" but the true meaning requires a full phrase in 
English to convey the subtleties and overtones: "revelling in another's 
misfortune."

The case in point is about how we should translate "Uebermensch". 
Literally, "Overman" is fine and it certainly conveys the idea. But for me, 
it is just too literal. The idea, (I believe; I am no expert on 
Nietzsche...) is the extension of Human power beyond where it is normally 
at, not with the idea of "lording it over" others, but to become the 
ultimate human possible. (A human in this state has rendered God redundant; 
hence God being "killed" by Humanity. It is as if God were a "collateral 
damage" casualty of the inexorable human struggle for growth... I really 
like this idea.) In this context, I think "Superman" is a more apt 
translation.

However, I would agree it is a matter of opinion and mine is not well 
informed on the subject.

Nevertheless, I did enjoy "Also sprach Zarathrustra" and I'll never be able 
to watch "2001 - A Space Odyssey" in quite the same way again... :-)

Thanks for the interesting and erudite background on the prefix, Doc.

Pete.
```

  Mostly, I found this interesting and (mostly) agree.  I was a little concerned 
by the statement,

word into Latin as 'super';  ..."

There are certainly MANY cases of things coming directly from  Greek to Latin,

that I am correct in concluding that the Greek and Latin "forms" both came from

```

Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
news:gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com...
> tlmfru wrote:
> > Hear, hear.  I'd suggest that once a person is aware of the reserved
words
> > for the language that there's very little to be gained.  Me, I'd prefer
> > unmixed case, lower or upper, but that's what Im used to.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> --


Quite so, no argument.  But "optimal" for any given person depends upon what
s/he's most used to reading - presumably literature, news, etc., in his or
her own language.  The obvious example is English vs. German - German
capitalizes all nouns (correct me if necessary on that) whereas English does
so much more sparingly - names, mostly.  Imagine your last paragraph being
written in pseudo-German style:

> Of course, That doesn't mean your Preference is "wrong" - just that
> You may prefer Something that's not optimal for Reading Speed and/or
> Accuracy. We choose to compromise Reading Speed, Ease, and Accuracy
> all the time for a Variety of Reasons.

For readers not used to it, the capital letters seem to imply extra
importance to the words - extra emphasis - which is certainly going to make
them slow up and blink at least occasionally; also that words are usually
capitalized in English at the beginning of the sentence might cause slight
problems.  I agree that with enough experience a person can become equally
fluent with programs written mixed-case following definite rules.  I suppose
the concept  "optimal" must include a time dimension as well.

From this it "follows" that unmixed case is optimal for me since I am most
used to reading English, and unmixed case is the closest to English style
(n.b., talking only of speed here).  You could argue that this means I ought
to capitalize all data names but I'll fall back on my comment about reserved
words.

PL
```

- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <%TUul.50873$Tp5.19525@newsfe13.iad> <gpltf3$ejb$2@reader1.panix.com> <gpm44702qqa@news6.newsguy.com> <gpm59i$qv9$1@reader1.panix.com> <sl_vl.42300$eT1.37513@newsfe20.iad> <gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com> <c4vwl.125100$EO2.119770@newsfe04.iad>`

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
> news:gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com...
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> s/he's most used to reading

That's a common assumption, but it's contradicted by the best
available evidence - empirical studies that use a variety of methods
to gauge reading success in terms of speed, accuracy, comprehension,
and effort. (Those methods range from timing and testing readers to
tracking their eye movements with various kinds of equipment, and
these days some researchers are even moving into using fNMRI analysis
of brain activity while people read.)

For example, people who read mostly magazines and newspapers are
generally most used to reading prose in narrow columns; but even those
readers have shown performance improvements with longer line lengths.

Until relatively recently, German newspapers were typically set in
Blackletter or a similar typeface. When they finally moved to a more
modern typeface, even long-time readers (who were used to reading text
set in Blackletter) found them easier to read.

> From this it "follows" that unmixed case is optimal for me since I am most
> used to reading English, and unmixed case is the closest to English style
> (n.b., talking only of speed here).

But English is my primary language as well, and I find mixed case more
readable than either monocase option. So other literacies can't have
that simple a relationship to source-code literacy.

This is just another example of the phenomenological gulf - we don't
actually know all that much about how we do what we do, and we don't
know that much about how much we don't know, and we often don't even
know we don't know it.
```

Very interesting!

Do any of these studies go into sentence length?  Or paragraph lengths?  It
drives me bonkers to have to read so much that is in
one-sentence-per-paragraph style; even moreso when it's 17 words or less per
sentence.  (I have lots of complaints about all these writing guides that
propound grade 6 rules).

Now here's a "theory" about reading speed & comprehension which I came up
with 10 minutes ago.  To wit:

- in any given font, upper-case letters are larger than lower-case.  (Are
there exceptions?)
- bigger letters are easier to read than smaller ones.
- THEREFORE, ALL WRITING SHOULD BE DONE IN UPPER-CASE BECAUSE IT'S EASIER TO
READ.

(Apologies to all of you who aren't aflicted with no-longer-youthful
eyesight!)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **In-Reply-To:** `<qf9xl.992$HF6.941@newsfe08.iad>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <%TUul.50873$Tp5.19525@newsfe13.iad> <gpltf3$ejb$2@reader1.panix.com> <gpm44702qqa@news6.newsguy.com> <gpm59i$qv9$1@reader1.panix.com> <sl_vl.42300$eT1.37513@newsfe20.iad> <gptsed0e4e@news3.newsguy.com> <c4vwl.125100$EO2.119770@newsfe04.iad> <gq0kv80i78@news4.newsguy.com> <qf9xl.992$HF6.941@newsfe08.iad>`

```
tlmfru wrote:
> 
> Do any of these studies go into sentence length?  Or paragraph lengths?

I imagine there are a number that do, though I can't cite any off the
top of my head. In the '40s and '50s, there were a lot of articles
that made recommendations about sentence length and such, but I'm not
sure how many of those were based on empirical data. (And I think eye
tracking studies came along later.)

Google Scholar found some interesting hits in ACM Journal of Computer
Documentation, but I'm not a SIGDOC member (at the moment - I probably
ought to join) so I can't read them at home. I'd have to be at school.

> It
> drives me bonkers to have to read so much that is in
> one-sentence-per-paragraph style; even moreso when it's 17 words or less per
> sentence.  (I have lots of complaints about all these writing guides that
> propound grade 6 rules).

Agreed. I'm dubious about style guides in general; they rarely seem to
be based on much more than the author's preferences, and even those
are often unexamined. (Richard Ohman did a great critique of the
Strunk and White commandment to "use concrete, specific language".)

> Now here's a "theory" about reading speed & comprehension which I came up
> with 10 minutes ago.  To wit:
> 
> - in any given font, upper-case letters are larger than lower-case.  (Are
> there exceptions?)

There are so many typefaces that you can find an exception to pretty
much any rule. Algerian, for example, has basically only one letter
height - but that's because it has essentially the same letterforms
for both cases. For some typefaces, such as Tunga, the difference in
letter height for the two cases is negligible. I don't think I have a
typeface installed on this machine that has lower-case letters that
are larger than upper-case ones, but it wouldn't surprise me if there
was one, especially among the "fantasy" typefaces (such as "graffiti"
fonts).

> - bigger letters are easier to read than smaller ones.

Are they? I just set the default font size in my browser to 72-point,
and it was pretty hard to read, mostly because very little text was
actually visible in the viewport.

True, the individual letters were still pretty visible. I bet that if
I set the x-height to, say, 1 mile, though, they would be tough to
read. At least until I get that really big monitor. (And then I'll
have to sit too far from the keyboard...)

> - THEREFORE, ALL WRITING SHOULD BE DONE IN UPPER-CASE BECAUSE IT'S EASIER TO
> READ.

Sure, all else being equal. But of course it isn't, so that doesn't
follow. :-)

I suspect - but have no data to support - that a majority of English
readers would find either mixed-case or all-lowercase easier to read
than all-uppercase, in the same typeface at the same letter height
(excluding bizarre fantasy typefaces).

I'm hypothesizing that based solely on the greater variety of
letterforms in lower- and mixed-case text; most typefaces have less
variety of form in uppercase, because uppercase letters tend to all be
the same height (while lowercase offer ascenders and descenders), have
less variation in width, have fewer opportunities for kerning, and
have fewer curves (contrast A and E with a and e, for example). Less
variety means fewer visual cues for letter recognition, maintaining
position on the line, etc.

But I could well be wrong. And there are different dimensions to
readability; readers might find all uppercase slower going, but have
improved accuracy or retention, for example (perhaps because the
unfamiliarity of all-uppercase prevented them from making as many
assumptions about the text).

And, of course, this could differ for other languages. Japanese
doesn't have case, but I wonder if anyone's compared readability of
hiragana and katakana.
```

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>> In article <%TUul.50873$Tp5.19525@newsfe13.iad>,
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Interesting and enjoyable. Thanks for the link.

While I wouldn't argue with what you said, it hinges on a single 
supposition: That what is written will be read.

That raises 2 further considerations:

1. What if people don't WANT to read it (can't be bothered... struggle with 
language anyway... etc...)?

As I am sure you are aware, there is a huge diversity in the way that Humans 
learn. Some people really struggle with language, but can look at intricate 
computer code and visualise it in an instant. Others (like me...) prefer to 
have a verbal description before getting down to nitty gritty.

2. What if there is never any NEED to read it?

I have some components which have not been maintained for over 10 years and 
are never likely to be. I don't get out the source code and read it for the 
entertainment value :-). There is an argument that says encapsulated 
functionality can be "write only"... (I have heard some uninformed people 
describe C as a "write only " language... don't even think about trying to 
decipher it in a year's time; just write the modified function again...)

(From personal experience, the very first C program I ever wrote that dealt 
with recursion over directory structures, was something I was quite proud of 
. I spent several days studying and testing until it worked. I was amazed at 
how few lines of code (compared to the COBOL  I was normally using) it took. 
It was very powerful with nested functionality within single statements. 18 
months later I needed to extend it and it was just a mystery. I spent half a 
day trying to figure out what it did and how it did it, then simply rewrote 
it... The experience made me think.)

In a COBOL environment where the source code is sacrosanct and large chunks 
of it must be maintained regularly, this idea seems to be Heresy. But I 
don't think it is. If the granularity of the system architecture is very 
small, encapsulated (pieces/units/bits of code/component methods/modules... 
or what have you), then it may indeed be quicker to simply rewrite without 
consulting the existing code. It might even be "desirable" if you don't want 
the previous approach to influence the new one...

Even if you DO consult the existing code, if it is small, it probably 
doesn't matter too much how "readable" it is.

I think the "rule" you quoted about having each idea on a single page is 
excellent. (Hadn't seen that before...). Certainly, I know that in C# the 
smaller and "tighter" the code, the easier it is to assimilate what it does 
quickly when uder pressure. I never thought I'd say this, but I DO find 
COBOL verbose now... It's like when someone is talking and you wish they'd 
get to the point.

> If I had to guess, I'd hypothesize that more practitioners would show
> best comprehension (fewer errors, faster reading time, or both) with
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't have any metrics for this either, but I "feel" it is probably 
true...

> But I don't know offhand of any empirical studies that have tested
> that. There probably are some, but I don't have 'em handy.

I noticed that APL was markedly missing from your discussion. I have seen 
people who are fluent in this do absolutely amazing things with a few 
seconds of typing. (I saw a demo done for my benefit where something that 
would have taken at least 500 lnes of COBOL required less than half a dozen 
lines in APL) However, what they write might as well be Swahili... I 
discussed this with a guy in Madrid who I am quite sure used to think in 
APL. He said he had always liked symbols and just had a natural flair for 
APL. (The point I mentioned above about different people having different 
affinities and facility across different learning channels.) He had no 
problem manipulating apparently abstract symbols, rather than words. I read 
an interview with Stephen Hawking where he said he visualises the problems 
he is dealing with using images and symbols and THEN "translates" the 
solution into standard math notation. In other words, he doesn't "think" in 
math...

The conclusions I would draw are as follows:

1. Programming (at least in most of the commonly used languages) IS 
technical writing. However, there may be some exclusions to this. (APL may 
be one...)
2. It is desirable to do what we can to make program code easily 
intelligible, WHETHER WE ARE LIKELY TO MAINTAIN IT OR NOT! BUT...  not at 
the expense of large amounts of writing that just add to the verbosity. 
There are "break even" points. Deciding what they are is part of the "Art" 
of computer programming; difficult to quantify.
3. Don't use APL. It is "dangerous" in every sense of the word... :-)

Pete.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Interesting and enjoyable. Thanks for the link.

Thanks. It was a fun presentation to write and deliver.

> While I wouldn't argue with what you said, it hinges on a single 
> supposition: That what is written will be read.

Yes, though I do talk a bit at the end about the economic drivers,
which are described in terms of the probability that code will be
read. It's true that not all code is read, but the available evidence
suggests that enough will to be worthwhile.

> 1. What if people don't WANT to read it (can't be bothered... struggle with 
> language anyway... etc...)?

Often a problem. Of course, sometimes there is no good alternative -
reading the code may be the only practical way of retrieving certain
information (eg about business rules that aren't well-documented, or
to do forensic analysis on a problem incident).

> 2. What if there is never any NEED to read it?

And yes, this comes up sometimes too. Of course, a component that's in
use and doesn't need to be maintained for 10 years might be worth
reading as an exemplar.

[snip some good examples of cases where reading the code might not be
necessary or desirable]

> I noticed that APL was markedly missing from your discussion.

Too far in the wrong direction.

I've done a bit of programming in APL, just to see what it was like. I
tend to agree with Djikstra: it encourages clever obscurantism. Dense
function composition, obscure symbolic tokens, right-to-left binding,
and so forth are actively hostile to human readers.

> I have seen
> people who are fluent in this do absolutely amazing things with a few 
> seconds of typing. (I saw a demo done for my benefit where something that 
> would have taken at least 500 lnes of COBOL required less than half a dozen 
> lines in APL) However, what they write might as well be Swahili...

Yes. I've seen a number of discussions by APL coders where they
decipher APL programs for entertainment. When reading code becomes a
puzzle-solving exercise, you have a maintenance problem.

APL's successor J, which uses ASCII, fewer symbols, and more words, is
better. But I don't think it's really usable as a general production
language. APL and J have some good ideas; the "function-oriented
programming" Backus describes is a nice alternative to LISP-style
functional programming. Those ideas need to be implemented in a
friendlier language, though.

> 1. Programming (at least in most of the commonly used languages) IS 
> technical writing. However, there may be some exclusions to this. (APL may 
> be one...)

Sure.

> 2. It is desirable to do what we can to make program code easily 
> intelligible, WHETHER WE ARE LIKELY TO MAINTAIN IT OR NOT! BUT...  not at 
> the expense of large amounts of writing that just add to the verbosity. 
> There are "break even" points. Deciding what they are is part of the "Art" 
> of computer programming; difficult to quantify.

Agreed, though that's true of technical writing as well. It's very
easy to produce a document that's too verbose for the reader.

> 3. Don't use APL. It is "dangerous" in every sense of the word... :-)

For just that reason, shouldn't everyone use it once? :-)
```

$IF 0
  Obfuscation Contest Entry
  Email to: oibcc@ptd.net
  Author: Michael Mattias, Sturtevant WI USA ( mcmattias@compuserve.com)
  Date: 5.02.98
  Requires: PowerBASIC/DOS version 3.2 or 3.5
  Rated: PG-13 (D)
$ENDIF
$LIB ALL OFF
DEFINT A-Z
DIM V AS BYTE PTR
READ N: REDIM Z(N-1) AS DWORD: DIM ABSOLUTE B(N*4-1) AS BYTE AT VARSEG 
(Z(0))
COLOR 7,1:CLS:V=pbvScrnBuff+1800
FOR I=0 TO N-1: READ Z(I):FOR J=0 TO 3:@V=B(I*4+J): INCR V:DELAY .05:INCR 
V:NEXT,
END
DATA 9,1936943435,544820512,544437057,1293971017,662266721
DATA 1917198451,544501359,1684957527,538998639

However, as a long time APLer (well, it was mostly in the 70s and early 80s 
I worked on APL...) I'd like to point out:

  It is possible to write obscurely in any language [and far too many 
programmers do so!]

One advantage that APL may have, in that regard, is use the conciseness of 
APL and its indifference to the structure of the data (vector, matrix, array 
etc) to carefully write code that is both readable and understandable.

It doesn't help unfortunately, when people publish idioms that showcase the 
obscure tricks that give APL the reputation it doesn't deserve.

I am also reminded of the old adage: 'dont use a hammer with screws' - eg: 
use the correct tool for the job. SO many shops/organizations pick the tools 
then go looking for jobs to do...

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: as the case may be

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-19T12:00:35-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gptsed1e4e@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gr7wl.22514$Ws1.1213@nlpi064.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<49AFF9C5.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <SXSul.120971$fM1.68482@newsfe14.iad> <7eb01b59-21f8-41cc-adf2-13b0d7527519@o11g2000yql.googlegroups.com> <%TUul.50873$Tp5.19525@newsfe13.iad> <gpltf3$ejb$2@reader1.panix.com> <gpm44702qqa@news6.newsguy.com> <728etmFodgjdU1@mid.individual.net> <gponjj21bol@news7.newsguy.com> <gr7wl.22514$Ws1.1213@nlpi064.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
Doug Miller wrote:
> In article <gponjj21bol@news7.newsguy.com>, Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote:
>> I've done a bit of programming in APL, just to see what it was like. I
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> the large majority of us whose native languages read left to right.

That's an interesting suggestion, but APL is typically written, and
meant to be read, left-to-right. Lines of APL source generally start
at the left edge of the screen, and so on.

Also, even in RTL languages, technical information (mathematical
formulas and so forth) are often written left-to-right. Japanese
prose, for example, is traditionally written vertically top-to-bottom,
with subsequent lines advancing from the right to the left (like
Chinese and Korean); but for technical documents, it's often written
horizontally, left-to-right, with lines advancing down. That makes it
easier to incorporate European-style mathematical notation.

So even people with an RTL first language might be more used to code
running left-to-right.

That's pure speculation on my part, though. I've met a few programmers
whose primary written language was RTL, but I don't know any well.
```

<a id="t-f63a7e121a"></a>
## Thread: <NOT> Invalid Key -"vs" Fie Status — 4 msgs

### <NOT> Invalid Key -"vs" Fie Status

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-05T17:52:13-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OAZrl.16871$UQ6.15548@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
(related to other posts)

For IBM shops, if you are working with VSAM KSDS files, it is also worth noting 
that when "checking" file status values (vs Invalid key) that you have access to 
the 2nd file status field (not to be confused with the 2nd byte of the Standard 
file status field).  Especially when debugging IBM "9x" values, this field may 
be very useful.  ON THE OTHER HAND, those values are fairly "obscure".  (I 
usually have a long search before I can even remember where they are 
documented).

As usual, "your mileage may vary".  The one thing that I would suggest (to IBM 
mainframe shops) is that *IF* you are using File Status fields, (for VSAM files) 
that you always DO define the 2nd field as well.
```

#### ↳ Re: <NOT> Invalid Key -"vs" Fie Status

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2009-03-06T09:55:03-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<59l2r4lpib3u5rg52hglsilmfsgod619a8@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<OAZrl.16871$UQ6.15548@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
On Thu, 5 Mar 2009 17:52:13 -0600, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote:

>For IBM shops, if you are working with VSAM KSDS files, it is also worth noting 
>that when "checking" file status values (vs Invalid key) that you have access to 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>documented).

In particular "97" is the status you care about:

01  PARM-FILE-STATUS        PIC X(02)   VALUE '00'.           
    88  PARM-FILE-STATUS-OK             VALUE '00' '10' '97'. 
    88  PARM-FILE-AT-END                VALUE '10'.           
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: <NOT> Invalid Key -"vs" Fie Status

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz
- **Date:** 2009-03-06T10:44:56-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4e786e0d-c042-40cb-bdcb-178238c661f7@s38g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<OAZrl.16871$UQ6.15548@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com> <59l2r4lpib3u5rg52hglsilmfsgod619a8@4ax.com>`

```
On Mar 7, 5:55 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Thu, 5 Mar 2009 17:52:13 -0600, "William M. Klein"
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> - James Madison

When testing a file status for success only the first byte should be
checked for '0'. The second byte may contain additional information.
There are several values defined in the standard wich are not '00' and
yet indicate success.

 01  PARM-FILE-STATUS        PIC X(02)   VALUE '00'.
     88  PARM-FILE-STATUS-OK             VALUE '00' THRU '09' '10'
'97'.
     88  PARM-FILE-AT-END                VALUE '10'.
```

<a id="t-dd04aedf0a"></a>
## Thread: status code 97 — 16 msgs

### status code 97

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-05T16:55:47-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49B00413.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Enterprise Cobol for z/OS...

Is there by chance an APAR or something that will make it so that a file
status of '97' (For VSAM only:  OPEN statement execution successful:  File
integrity verified.) is implicitly converted to a file status of '00'?

Although documented in the VSE Cobol manual, I have never in my life seen
this status returned when opening a VSAM file on VSE.

z/OS is another matter.  I just got my first one.  (The last job that used
the file was cancelled, obviously causing the need for "integrity
verification" when we reran the program.)

Because we've never seen it, none of our programs are coded to expect it.

Since I could not care less if the "file integrity was verified" I'd just as
soon receive the '00' status code, rather than making changes to hundreds of
programs to handle status '97' as being OK!

Ideas?

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: status code 97

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-03-06T02:04:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<goq0b4$1n6$2@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<49B00413.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
In article <49B00413.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>,
Frank Swarbrick <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:
>Enterprise Cobol for z/OS...

[snip]

>Since I could not care less if the "file integrity was verified" I'd just as
>soon receive the '00' status code, rather than making changes to hundreds of
>programs to handle status '97' as being OK!
>
>Ideas?

1) Make the solution JCL-dependent, eg run an IDCAMS VERIFY step before 
each program-running VSAM-using step.

2) Change the copybook that is part of your system standard for FILE 
STATUS checking to include 88  GOOD-IO VALUES '00' '97'.

('But... those solutions require... work!')

DD
