According to http://babelfish.altavista.com - which,in my 
browser,redirects to http://babelfish.yahoo.com, how things change, my 
word, indeed - it gets rendered as 'Why simply make when man can 
complicate?'... but Mr Dashwood;s translation should suffice.

>
>I get irritated when I hear someone (usually a Manager) using the acroynm 
>"KISS" (Keep It Simple, Stupid) to rally the troops.

It has been long, long time since someone has tried to pull that one on 
me.  My response used to be 'It isn't really that difficult, let me show 
you what it is *really* doing...' and then compile with a LIST option and 
say 'Now here,s what's *really* going on...' ... and wait until the eyes 
glazed over and then I'd never be bothered by that person again.

Nowadays I don't get much of it, though... and when I do I remind my 
client that making something simple is very, *very* hard work and you do 
realise that asking for more of my work means means signing more of my 
timesheets, right?

I know I am a consultant/contractor/hired gun and no matter how good my 
work is the moment I leave anything that goes wrong will be blamed on 
me... it is De Rererum Natura in this (and many other) crafts, it's always 
the fault of the guy who just left.  I work very, very hard, though... 
including comments like *** KIDS, DON'T TRY THIS AT HOME *** and *** 
WARNING: TWO-YEAR PROGRAMMER CODE STOP HERE, CONTINUE WITH CAUTION *** so 
that I hope to have, some day, a corner-office idiot look at my work and 
say 'But... but it is so *simple*, why did we pay him so much money to 
write such simple stuff?'... and not realise that the difficulty came from 
making the stuff simple, to eliminate reference modifications from 
copymembers that weren't designed to do what they wanted and submit them 
along for the Prod move, to break down a ADD that went on for pages into 
four COMPUTES (that still go on for pages... but the chunks got 
smaller)... little things.

Meanwhile, in the latest newse... we've gotten a new Corner Office Idiot, 
our... where's my octal hand?... sixth in two years... but he seems to 
like me and has extended my contract, originally a 'nine months at the 
most' job begun in November, 2003, for another four months.

(I actually dodged an offer of a full-time position rather well, even by 
my own standards... the COI made one, I burbled some 'How very kind of 
you, let me muse over this' noises'... waited a bit... and then, like all 
the other Corner Office Idiots, she was gone.  When my Tech Lead mentioned 
it, long after the position had expired, I smiled and said 'Come now, Mr 
Jones... Ms Smith did not offer me a full-time job, considering what we 
both know about me we know that she offered me an insult.  Wasn't I 
polite?'

He laughed, ruefully, and said 'Yeah... but if that's all she had to 
offer...'... and I responded with 'If that's what she has to offer then 
this is what she gets in return; it is good that I am calm and do not take 
umbrage at such insults, eh?'

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Call a .BAT file?

- **From:** spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
- **Date:** 2009-03-14T16:46:29+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ojQul.21840$yr3.1440@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<49b888db$0$5478$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <GJsul.22159$Ws1.5311@nlpi064.nbdc.sbc.com> <gpdlj1$g36$3@reader1.panix.com> <7206l1FmsavuU1@mid.individual.net> <gpfpmc$cba$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
In article <gpfpmc$cba$1@reader1.panix.com>, docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>In article <7206l1FmsavuU1@mid.individual.net>,
>Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>>>
>>> As the Italians used to say, 'Warum einfach machen wann Mann kann
>>> kompliziert?'

Should be "komplizieren"; "kompliziert" means "complicated".

Should also be "mann", not "Mann". In lower-case, it's a pronoun meaning "one, 
someone, anyone, generic 'you', etc.". In upper-case, it's a noun meaning 
"man, mankind".

The word order is wrong, too: should be "... wann mann komplizieren kann."

"Einfach" can be translated as either "simple" or "simply"; inserting "etwas" 
("something") after "Warum" removes the ambiguity of meaning.

>>
>>[for the benefit of non-German speakers: "Why make things simple, when you 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>complicate?'... but Mr Dashwood;s translation should suffice.

The babelfish translation is a fairly accurate translation of what you 
[incorrectly] *wrote*; Mr. Dashwood's translation accurately captures what I 
believe you *meant*.
```

Michael Mattias wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:71tmc0Fn6i3nU1@mid.individual.net...
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> MCM

Yes, I think so.

I have a theory that all intelligent people are lazy. (It doesn't mean that 
all lazy people are intelligent :-))

If being smart doesn't help us find easier, better, more effiecient, ways to 
do things, then there's not much point in being smart.

The Victorian work ethic embodied in the "stairs to success" is a noble one 
and will work whether you are smart or not, but it is the nature of smart 
people to look for an "easier" way.

6 years ago, here in this very forum, Judson and I were debating the merits 
of  "good" and "bad" code. There were some very long posts and I offer the 
following extract from one of them...:

<anecdote on the side - skip this if you are of a serious disposition and
are only interested in the debate...>


Many years ago, before my body was wrecked by a dissolute, self indulgent
lifestyle, I used to climb mountains.


(Hanging on to a rock face by your fingernails is excellent practice for
handling IT deadlines, but I didn't know that at the time <G>)


Anyway, the guy who was my climbing teacher was one of the wild breed of
Kiwis who live ONLY for mountains. He belonged to the same club as Edmund
Hillary and was of similar disposition.


On one occasion we were working our way up a ridge on Taranaki (Mt. Egmont,
8260 feet...believe me I have counted every one of them several
times...<G>), when we came to a sheer outcrop of rock called "Humphrey's
Castle".  Noticing there was a well worn path around this obstacle, and not
being entirely stupid, I started to walk around it. Bob, my teacher, pulled
me up and said: "Whered'ja think yer goin'?". I replied that there was an
established path around it and it made sense to take the low risk approach.
He looked at me with piercing blue eyes that showed just the faintest hint
of scorn, and said: "Peter, let's pretend this is the ONLY way..."  To my
surprise we scaled it, and I was rewarded with a smile from Bob for my
efforts, and a sandwich on the top of it. The view was worth the climb, and
the lesson has never been forgotten over the ensuing years.


To this day, whenever I hear the phrase: "the only way..." I think about a
summer afternoon sitting on Humphries Castle gazing down a sheer rock face
several thousand feet into green valleys below, and realise that "the only
way" is NOT the only way; it is the only way we can think of at the moment,
and sometimes, the hard way is the most rewarding.


</anecdote on the side - skip this if you are of a serious disposition and
are only interested in the debate...>

As you can see, I am not averse to doing things that are hard, but (and I 
find this increasingly as I get older) I will ALWAYS look for an easier way.

I don't think there's anything wrong with this and I'm quite sure we 
wouldn't have most of the labour saving devices we take for granted every 
day, if there weren't other people out there who feel the same.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Call a .BAT file?

- **From:** "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net>
- **Date:** 2009-03-12T09:01:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49b915b6$0$5453$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<71se0pFmonffU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49b888db$0$5478$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <71se0pFmonffU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Thanks Pete.  I am using scripts in the .BAT file to initiate an FTP.  My 
Cobol program 1st modifies the .BAT file to put yesterday's date into the 
name of the file to be retrieved, then FTP's it, then processes the file for 
it's intended business purpose.  Since the .BAT file works, "if it ain't 
broke, don't fix it".  Thanks again, Paul


"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:71se0pFmonffU1@mid.individual.net...
Paul H wrote:
> I want to have my program execute a .BAT file, then continue to
> process. Call and cancel doesn't work.  I'm using an old version of
> MicroFocus COBOL. Is there a method to accomplish this?  TIA, Paul

You can get your COBOL program to execute ANY executable (including a .BAT
file) by using the Windows Shell API. It is exactly the same as running a
DOS command.

You need the ShellExecute  or WinExec commands of the Windows API. If you
are running an old (16 bit) compiler it is in a .dll called "shell.dll", if
you are running something better than windows 95 it is in a .dll called
"shell32.dll"

Here's a link to sample code:

http://supportline.microfocus.com/examplesandutilities/nesamp.asp#Win32API

If you substitute your .Bat filename into the WinExec sample, where it says
"Notepad.exe"  you should be OK.

WinExec is fine to kick off something simple, but you should really use the
ShellExecuteEX if you are going to handle documents.

Check out both these samples for future reference.

(BTW, .bat files are really obsolete and best avoided. These days it is .wsh
(Windows Script Hosting), which has much richer functionality,  is closer to
the system, and much more stable. You can do much more than was ever
possible with .bat files. Depending on what you want your batch file to do,
you should consider writing it in WSH or VBScript. (You can also use
JavaScript or C# if you want to get really sexy :-))

HTH,

Pete
```

I know what you mean.  When I went to DeVry in about 1960, they trained us a 
little in analog computer technology (using 400 volt DC and tubes, as I 
remember, with circuits that measured voltages to determine results of 
division, etc. to a somewhat accurate answer - sort of like an electronic 
slide rule).  They said there were digital computers but they would never go 
anywhere.  We also fooled around a little with transistors, but mostly built 
full-wave ??? vacuum tube power supplies.  And, I'm probably getting about 
what I'm worth for the Cobol work - but I can do it at home, so my commute 
is walking upstairs in my 'jammies...  Keeps the semi-retired 
getting-feeble-minded folks like me busy.

there might be more than one way to interpret what I wrote.

>Aww
>shucks!  And I've only been programming in various languages (10 or 15) for
>a few years (since 1963) so I really want to keep current on EVERYTHING!

Best to take it easy, then... I've noticed a trend towards replacing good,
solid vacuum-tubes ('valves' in Britspeak) with thingummies based on the
work of William Shockley (and others), I think they're called
'transistors'.

>I'm doing Cobol because I have a contracting job for a company that can't
>hardly find anyone who understands their systems.

I've noticed that companies who say 'we can't find hardly nobody who can
(x)' are usually saying 'we can't find hardly nobody who can (x) FOR THE
PRICE WE WANT TO PAY'; my advice is 'double the rate you're offering and
see what kind of responses you get.'

>Thanks, Paul

You're quite welcome... and it was worth at least *double* what I charged
you for it!

DD
```

```
On Mar 12, 5:00 pm, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoS...@att.net> wrote:
> I want to have my program execute a .BAT file, then continue to process.
> Call and cancel doesn't work.  I'm using an old version of MicroFocus COBOL.
> Is there a method to accomplish this?  TIA, Paul

CALL "SYSTEM" USING command-string

The command string should be null terminated.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol monograph hypothesis suggestions

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-13T12:58:47+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<71tltoFmnv67U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<c58ea716-a160-4129-9657-573a65fc2710@h5g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Anthony Aquino wrote:
> Hi guys,
> can you help me with some possible hypothesis for my cobol monograph?
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks!

Hi Anthony,

from the mail I receive, it seems that COBOL is alive and well in South
America. I know that several of the "how to" type papers I have written have
appeared on South American web sites (some in Brazil).

It might help us if you explained exactly what your "COBOL monograph" is,
and what it is for.

Is this something you are doing as part of a University or Technical College
course, or what is it that is required?

If you are required to write something extolling the virtues of COBOL you
will need to be careful. Standard COBOL (procedural) is in its twilight; the
future belongs to objects and layers. OO COBOL may have a place, but my
personal opinion is that even that place is limited.(The competition is too
fierce and other OO languages do it better...)

As for being able to make a living writing COBOL (as Doc advised) I really
couldn't agree with that. Sure, it is possible for us old timers, but I
wouldn't (couldn't in all conscience) advise a young person to get into
COBOL as a way to make a living any more. It is useful to have knowledge of
it, but make sure you have other skills as well.

In the last 15 years, the market for COBOL skills has shrunk by over 85%
(figures based on JobServe, a major European online placement service.)
While this may not reflect the situation in the place where you live, it is
still worth being aware of. You need more useful skills than COBOL if you
intend to be a career programmer in this century.

If you want to find some unique factor for your monograph, I don't think
portability is a really good one. Migrating COBOL across platforms is far
from straightforward. (Ask anyone who's done it...) yes, at a core level
COBOL is portable, but, in reality, people use vendor extensions and these
make it less so. It does not have the portability that Java has, for
example.

Consider the strengths that COBOL DOES have... it is easy to maintain (when
written well) but this is countered by the requirement to maintain thousands
of lines of it. (The cost of labour intensive maintenance has been a major
factor in the decline of COBOL.)

It has EXCELLENT I/O and data manipulation facilities. This is one reason
why it is likely to remain the best option for batch processing for the
foreseeable future.

It is easy to learn (even for people without a computer science background)
and you can achieve results pretty quickly with it while you are learning.

Please tell us more about what you are needing/trying to do.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol monograph hypothesis suggestions

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-19T12:08:59-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gptsee2e4e@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<8hd3s4tfou3cbd702ehqubc5jj4jigk7ud@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<c58ea716-a160-4129-9657-573a65fc2710@h5g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <Ls-dnUhW4cZQm1zUnZ2dnUVZ_srinZ2d@earthlink.com> <8hd3s4tfou3cbd702ehqubc5jj4jigk7ud@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Wed, 18 Mar 2009 09:35:56 -0500, "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> I suspect the supporters of FORTRAN might disagree.

I couldn't tell if HeyBub meant that COBOL was the first language with
all three of those attributes, or simply that it was the first
language "put into general use" regardless of points 2 and 3.

The former assertion is correct; the latter is not. FORTRAN went into
general use a couple of years before COBOL did, and LISP around the
same time as COBOL.

The first FORTRAN compiler appeared in 1958. The first LISP
interpreter was probably running in late 1958, and the language went
public in 1960. CODASYL released the first COBOL specification in
1960. But neither FORTRAN nor LISP was designed by a committee (at
least not formally; Backus and McCarthy didn't work alone), and
neither was developed under the aegis of the DoD.
```

## Thread: The price of success... Firefox users take note. — 5 msgs

### The price of success... Firefox users take note.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-13T15:43:22+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<71tvibFmv73bU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
After getting feedback from people here about the Cobdata site, I have 
switched to using Firefox as the default Browser for Web development. I 
still use IE as my default Browser, but I also ensure that all pages are 
working in Firefox before publishing them.

Firefox is an excellent product and I like a number of the features in it, 
but I still feel more comfortable with IE.

Just in case there is any smugness about Firefox's "superior lack of 
vulnerability" you might like to be aware of this...

http://www.pcadvisor.co.uk/news/index.cfm?NewsID=108016

I have always claimed that one reason why IE has been targeted has been 
because it is successful, and not because of any particular innate 
vulnerability. My contention is that EVERYTHING is vulnerable...The more 
successful somethng becomes, the more chance of it being targeted.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: The price of success... Firefox users take note.

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-16T12:40:37-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gpm44712qqa@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<71tvibFmv73bU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<71tvibFmv73bU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> I have always claimed that one reason why IE has been targeted has been 
> because it is successful, and not because of any particular innate 
> vulnerability.

That is indeed one reason. Alas, there are others, including a great
many particular innate vulnerabilities.

The simple fact is that IE 6 (and to a lesser extent some of the other
IE releases) has a great many features which are insecure as designed.
Other mainstream browsers have smaller attack surfaces simply by
virtue of omitting those features.

> My contention is that EVERYTHING is vulnerable...The more
> successful somethng becomes, the more chance of it being targeted.

The former is a truism - you can always extend a threat model past the
security parameters of a system. (Not many systems are designed to
resist a massive asteroid strike.) The latter is difficult to prove,
however plausible; but doesn't it imply that the makers of the
most-successful browser have an obligation to devote extra attention
to its security?
```

<a id="t-5ec53eb872"></a>
## Thread: OpenCOBOL, Vala, GPS and limitless potential — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Open-source COBOL (GnuCOBOL, OpenCOBOL, TinyCOBOL)`](topics.md#open-source)_

### OpenCOBOL, Vala, GPS and limitless potential

- **From:** btiffin@canada.com
- **Date:** 2009-03-14T21:02:47-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1fec7d88-1f1a-4b41-a11c-2591a96eca10@j35g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello everyone,

Recent developments in OpenCOBOL

"Discovered" Vala, a programming language in the style of c-sharp
without the gunk.  Designed for easing the use of GNOME libraries, but
keeping with a C ABI.  As it turns out, this is excellent fit for
OpenCOBOL extension writing.   http://live.gnome.org/Vala

For instance, a sample embedded WebKit based browser in 120ish lines
of Vala and some 12 lines of OpenCOBOL, mainly a simple CALL passing
the URL.  The browser window sports forward, back and refresh buttons
along with a location bar, and as it's the WebKit engine also sports
excellent support for the most sophisticated sites.

Screen shot of the browser creating a post on the opencobol.org forums
at http://add1tocobol.com/tiki-browse_image.php?imageId=14

Screen shot of the browser viewing the screen shot of the browser at
http://add1tocobol.com/tiki-browse_image.php?imageId=15

OpenCOBOL has already easily linked to many of the Vala GTK+ and glib
samples; Text entry, Text Viewer, Poppler PDF viewer, Glade based tree
views.  This was all possible with the C code that was a work in
progress before, but Vala reduces the time and code needed by one or
two orders of magnitude.  Quite exciting.

With this new tool accelerating the GTK+ linkage and opening a
plethora of other Glib features, attention turned to an IDE.  I can't
speak for everyone, but Eclipse doesn't fly fast enough on my
development box so I never got far enough to try the COBOL plug-in.
Enter GNAT Programming Studio.

GPS is working out as a very nice development environment.  Yes it Ada
centric, but it's extensible feature set is allowing the smooth
integration of OpenCOBOL, C and Vala.

I can't help myself when I get into early days of efforts, and a
screen shot of the cobc control panel (incomplete) is at
http://add1tocobol.com/tiki-browse_image.php?imageId=18

A shot of the style of the editors syntax highlighter and the
OpenCOBOL menu in action at
http://add1tocobol.com/tiki-browse_image.php?imageId=17

GPS also supports text expansion aliases and the current opencobol.xml
customization includes a handy one.  Type "program", hit Ctrl-O and it
fills in an empty OpenCOBOL template ready for fleshing out.  Screen
captures of the before and after at
http://add1tocobol.com/tiki-browse_image.php?imageId=19
and
http://add1tocobol.com/tiki-browse_image.php?imageId=20

All this and things have barely started; some 350 lines of GPS XML
customization (including syntax highlighter rules for OpenCOBOL and
Vala).  And Ada itself includes external access rules for COBOL in
it's specification, so using GNAT as a jumping off point doesn't seem
like an overly bad thing.  The OpenCOBOL FAQ already has a sample
source code for interfacing to GNAT Ada and this tool brings the two a
little closer.

As as aside; OpenCOBOL should have sample how-to links for gretl (Gnu
Regression, Econometrics and Time-series Library) and Octave numerical
analysis soon, and there are plans to include the syntax in GPS
customizing files.

Given the three tools of OpenCOBOL, Vala and GPS (along with
OpenCOBOL's easy access to all things based on the C application
binary interface) there could well be some spiffy looking GUI, network
aware, feature rich yet secure, open source, small and medium business
applications coming our way very soon.

Free and open COBOL is something to watch in my humble opinion and,
the reason for the post really, get involved in.

Cheers,
Brian
```

---

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: DATABASE CONNECTIVITY IN COBOL WITH VISUAL STUDIO 2008

- **From:** Rene_Surop <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2009-03-15T17:47:58-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<71e9f553-9106-4146-8fee-b239d4e75f49@f1g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<2bf18188-341f-4b33-82cf-25aca834a0ba@z8g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <avbqr41lgvno2lph10b7kop498ev97at0q@4ax.com> <da426858-ff21-4e0b-a7fa-b6592d7e867b@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`

```
       ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
       SPECIAL-NAMES.
           CALL-CONVENTION 74 IS winAPI.

                :
                :

       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01  wValues.
           05  wSQLstat                PIC s9(9) comp-5.
               88 sqlSuccess            VALUES 0 1.
           05  wMy-flags               pic 9(8) comp-5.
           05  wMy-retcode             pic 9(8) comp-5.

       01  connString                  pic x(256) value
            'Driver={MySQL ODBC 5.1 Driver};Server=localhost;'
           &'Port=3306;Database=hotelDB;User=root;'
           &'Password=mypassword;Option=3;'.

       EXEC SQL INCLUDE SQLCA END-EXEC

                :
                :

       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
           PERFORM 000-Connect-toSQL


           STOP RUN.
                :
                :

       000-Connect-toSQL SECTION.
           EXEC SQL
             CONNECT TO :connString
           END-EXEC.

           MOVE SQLCODE TO wSQLstat

           IF sqlSuccess
             CONTINUE
           ELSE
             CALL winAPI 'MessageBoxA' USING
               BY VALUE 0 SIZE 4,
               BY REFERENCE ".Error : 003." & x'0D0A' &
                ".SQL-BASED data disconnection." & x'0D0A' &
                ".Call MIS group or vendor for support."
               BY REFERENCE z"My Company dotCOM"
               BY VALUE wMy-flags
               RETURNING wMy-retcode
             END-CALL
           END-IF.



Simple code to open a MySQL ODBC database. Search "ODBC connection
string" in Google (as Pete's advised).
```

ODBC. If you say you are using openESQL in MF, then create an ODBC DSN
in your Windows XP "ODBC Data Source Administrator". Click "Add"
button and create one using your ODBC data engine. once generated....
you can now use the created ODBC DSN name in MF openESQL.

I am using MF NetExpress myself.

- **In-Reply-To:** `<60ad37b2-ce9a-424f-b85e-69d0f10900cb@w24g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<2bf18188-341f-4b33-82cf-25aca834a0ba@z8g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <avbqr41lgvno2lph10b7kop498ev97at0q@4ax.com> <da426858-ff21-4e0b-a7fa-b6592d7e867b@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <71e9f553-9106-4146-8fee-b239d4e75f49@f1g2000prb.googlegroups.com> <0baef5dc-913d-4057-bd5c-a48d5d373914@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <ac666e92-4e12-4a7c-a476-71848a6633ef@e36g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <60ad37b2-ce9a-424f-b85e-69d0f10900cb@w24g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`

```
ammasakthisridhar@rediffmail.com wrote:

It's bit easier now that we know you are using Net Express 5.1 in a .Net 
environment. From what I read you are trying to turn a PROCEDURAL based 
application (CONSOLE = SCREEN SECTION ????), into a full-bloodied 
Internet On-Line Hotel Reservation system.

For starters, as Rene recommended DON'T mix you old 'console' 
application with a .Net Internet application.

Are you going too fast ? Have you read in detail what Micro Focus gives 
you about OpenESQL. Go to the on-line manuals for 5.1 :-

http://supportline.microfocus.com/documentation/books/nx51ws01/nx51indx.htm

and specifically read through Chapter 8 of the Database manual which 
covers the topic. I only have N/E V 3.1,  but as you have 5.1 where the 
text gives you a 'How to ...' button you can access their examples.

Associated with the ESQL Assistant, do you have an ODBC Driver installed 
for SQL Server 2000. The steps are :-

1 - design DB tables per Server 2000
2 - register the ODBC Driver before attempting ESQL Assistant.
3 - ESQL Asst. will give you a Treeview of DB systems (ODBC drivers) 
installed and relevant DB files; these you can expand in the Treeview to 
access particular tables.
4 - Start small - a table with a few fields/columns. Get the feel of the 
ESQL Asst. tool by generating a query. The Assistant will give you a set 
of results without exiting the Assistant or having to compile any 
programs. Get the hang of the equivalent of Add, Change, Delete etc., or 
  searching using criteria.

Bear in mind you don't necessarily need to use cursors for look-ups. 
Some DBs allow you to use 'CURSOR WITH HOLD' and you can also use a 
fixed Query-Table size to hold results of queries without using cursors.

WITH HOLD :
-----------
     *> Test for HOLD cursor
     exec sql declare curhold cursor with hold for
         select col1, col2, col3 from mftest
             order by col1
     end-exec

Note : I have MS Access 2003 - so I know from the M/F test program  that 
the above 'with hold' won't work; perhaps it does with MS Access 2007. 


HOST ARRAYS :
-------------

78 MaxTimes             value 25.

  EXEC SQL BEGIN DECLARE SECTION END-EXEC
   01  Descrip-Rec-Table.
       03 A-DesID        occurs MaxTimes  PIC X(2).
       03 A-DesCode      occurs MaxTimes  PIC X(4).
       03 A-DesName      occurs MaxTimes  PIC X(40).
       03 A-DesOther1    occurs MaxTimes  PIC X(10).
       03 A-DesOther2    occurs MaxTimes  PIC X(10).
       03 A-DesUpdated   occurs MaxTimes  PIC X(16).
  EXEC SQL END DECLARE SECTION END-EXEC.

  EXEC SQL
   SELECT
          `A`.`DesID`
         ,`A`.`DesCode`
         ,`A`.`DesName`
         ,`A`.`DesOther1`
         ,`A`.`DesOther2`
         ,`A`.`DesUpdated`
   INTO
        :A-DesID:A-DesID-NULL
       ,:A-DesCode:A-DesCode-NULL
       ,:A-DesName:A-DesName-NULL
       ,:A-DesOther1:A-DesOther1-NULL
       ,:A-DesOther2:A-DesOther2-NULL
       ,:A-DesUpdated:A-DesUpdated-NULL
     FROM `Descriptions` A
  END-EXEC

display sqlerrd(3) 	*> Count in Table will be from a value of zero
			*> to 25 entries

---------------------------------------------------------------
Plug away at the ESQL Assistant until you are comfortable with its features.

Assuming you are working with ESQL in COBOL write a COBOL Procedural or 
OO Program to access the entry types and get results. Are you using .Net 
BASIC for 'screening' (GUIs) ? If so then you'll need to pass from COBOL 
to BASIC via .Net. (Can't help you here as I don't have .Net).

Bear in mind, if you application is totally COBOL-based, no use of C# or 
BASIC from .Net etc., then you can use V 5.1 in a COBOL Environment ( 
i.e., the Net Express IDE - and your GUI-ing is done through N/E OO 
Classes or Dialog System)). It's only if you are going to use other 
languages that you need to have, (or rather, it is DESIRABLE to have), 
your application in .Net with Visual Studio.

```
In article <2d710d19-d201-4de0-bd2a-d48b9b8d0fab@p36g2000prp.googlegroups.com>,
 <ammasakthisridhar@rediffmail.com> wrote:
>sir,
>
>i have already told that in my project i dont have any room concept
>but i have various types of rooms such as normal,deluxe and so on.

From the start, then, it seems as though there will be difficulties.  
System design, as I was taught, is *not* a 'one size fits all' thing; in 
your case a six-room bed-and-breakfast will benefit from a system designed 
differently than a system designed for a 120-room hotel-by-the-airport, 
the 120-short-stay system will be of different design than one for a 
500-unit resort-by-the-sea.

First, you determine types of transactions, then you determine transaction 
flow, then you determine user interface - a customer doing online bookings 
might see/think of things differently than a reservations clerk at a 
toll-free number for a major chain - *then* you begin to design.

>
>i dont have any money sir sorry sir.

You will get from me, at least, double what you've paid for.  I do this 
for a living.

[snip]

>please it is very urgent sir.

The more urgent the project, the better the case can be made for increased 
budget... up to a point, of course.  No matter how hard you try I do not 
think you will be able to hire nine women to have a single, full-term baby 
in one month.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: DATABASE CONNECTIVITY IN COBOL WITH VISUAL STUDIO 2008

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Alain Reymond <arwebmail@skynet.be>
- **Date:** 2009-03-19T23:04:27+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49c2c1ab$0$2855$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<97938742-b004-4679-abe7-13ba7b08be6c@i20g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<2bf18188-341f-4b33-82cf-25aca834a0ba@z8g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <gpltl8$ejb$3@reader1.panix.com> <893185e3-07ff-48db-8f63-e11675861397@d2g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <gpm5ck$qv9$2@reader1.panix.com> <gponje01bol@news7.newsguy.com> <97938742-b004-4679-abe7-13ba7b08be6c@i20g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello,

If you are interested in coding in cobol (as mentioned earlier) and have
MF NetExpress 5.1 (also as mentioned before), you have all tools to
develop you project. It is a question of methodology.
It seems that you have a running cobol application but want to connect
it to a database :
o  does the database exist ? if no :
      i.  have a look at your cobol application and see if the files can
be easily translated to a database structure
      ii. if they are close to a "normalized" (in the way of a Codd
relational definition), the job will be quite easy
      iii. if not, try to make the modification to normalize the file
structure as much as possible and have the application running under cobol.
o  design a database structure which will be a clone of your cobol file
system
o  under XP with the administration tools, create an ODBC definition to
access the database server (MySQL, MS-SQL, Access, Postgres, whatever)
o  use OpenESQL Assistant delivered with NetExpress 5.1 to create all
cobol connections and queries to the database through ODBC (many
examples come with NetExpress)
o  replace the read/write access in your application with Embedded SQL
orders and adapt the program as necessary.

This does not work 100% and depends largely on the structure of your
existing cobol code. But it can be quite a quick process to access the
data stored in a database without rewriting a complete application.

Regards,

Alain


ammasakthisridhar@rediffmail.com a ï¿½crit :
> sir,
> it is an ordinary hotel such as normal,single bed,double bed and so
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

## Thread: .XLS, not .CSV from NETX? — 9 msgs

### .XLS, not .CSV from NETX?

- **From:** "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net>
- **Date:** 2009-03-24T13:16:42-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49c923a1$0$5503$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
I create .CSV files in my Net Express programs, then my users can open them 
with Excel.  But the column widths, bold, line sizes etc. are not set.  I'd 
like to create a .XLS file that has these characteristics built in.  Is this 
possible?  I only know COBOL, not C or VB or etc.  TIA, Paul
```

#### ↳ Re: .XLS, not .CSV from NETX?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-25T10:34:14+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<72t1ulFs08c7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49c923a1$0$5503$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> I create .CSV files in my Net Express programs, then my users can
> open them with Excel.  But the column widths, bold, line sizes etc.
> are not set.  I'd like to create a .XLS file that has these
> characteristics built in.  Is this possible?  I only know COBOL, not
> C or VB or etc.  TIA, Paul

The properties you describe are part of the receptacle, not the contents.

You create the contents and these are loaded into a default container.

Try setting up an Excel spreadsheet with the attributes you want (line/col 
width, bold, etc) then save it as a template.

This should give you the .XLS you are looking for.

Alternatively, you can use Automation (Excel as a COM componenet) to access 
Excel directly from your program and set the attributes you want , then load 
the data directly (without needing the .CSV) or run a macro to load it, all 
under program control. There are many ways to do what you are trying to do.

If you want the users to initiate the Excel stuff, and you want it to look 
as you intended, then provide a template for them to use.

HTH,

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: .XLS, not .CSV from NETX?

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: .XLS, not .CSV from NETX?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-26T12:31:59+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<72vt7hFskdjbU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49c923a1$0$5503$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <72t1ulFs08c7U1@mid.individual.net> <49c993a0$0$5460$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> Thanks, Pete, is this what you had in mind?
> http://user.services.openoffice.org/en/forum/viewtopic.php?f=25&p=77541
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

No, this isn't what I had in mind. I found it overwhelming, too :-)

This is a post from a guy who wants to move OFF Excel to Open Office. He 
already has a number of  programs using Automation to Excel and is wondering 
if there is an API in Open Office that can support the same Classes, 
Methods, and Properties. He's looking foir a COM interface to Open Office.

I have no idea whether there is one, but I'd be surprised if it supports the 
same method calls as Excel.

Anyway, this is way too difficult a route for you to try if you are not into 
OO COBOL (although I admire your courage and attitude... :-))

Fortunately, for what you need to do you only need a template. You can 
provide this to the users and tell them to use it when they import your 
.CSV.

That will get the job done.

HOWEVER...

If you DO want to get into program control and use COM Automation (which 
allows you to connect to and control most of the Office Suite (I'm not sure 
about Power Point... never done it), including MS Access and Excel you 
should find  a list of the properties and methods on MSDN. Of course a very 
easy way is to use an Object Browser to look at Excel. You already have one 
on your system to support VBA macros... It's a bit hard to explain without 
posting screenshots, but try this (Description is for Excel 2007; tabs and 
menus may be different in other versions...):

1. Open Excel.

2. Select the "Developer" tab.

3. Click the icon to open Visual Basic. The Visual Basic Editor opens and 
displays a toolbar with small icons on it. There should be one that looks 
like an open cardboard box with 3 shapes floating over it. This is the 
Object Browser. (The toolbar icons have tooltips so floating the mouse over 
it should confirm if you have the right icon.) Alternatively try pressing F2 
while on the Editor screen., that should open the Object Browser

4. Change the drop down that says <All Libraries> to be Excel. Immediately 
you can see all the classes that Excel supports in the left pane, and all 
the Methods, Properties and Events for the selected class in the right pane. 
Note that properties have a hand holding a page icon, methods have what 
looks like a green eraser, and events have a little lightning flash. To get 
more information about any one of these, select it, then click the question 
mark icon near the top of the screen, or click  Help.

I wrote a number of components in Fujitsu COBOL that interfaced to EXCEL and 
ACCESS through COM Automation and found it very powerful.The experience 
gained was useful because now I do it from C#. The only source of 
information I used was the VB Object Browser, as described above.

The number of methods and properties available is indeed overwhelming, but 
you'd end up using a tiny subset of them to achieve most purposes.

As the COBOL man in the post you quoted re Open Office said: "I am on my 
own".

My experience using COBOL was exactly that. Most of the examples and sample 
code are in VB and, increasingly, C#. I taught myself VB so Icould 
understand the bloody samples, then translate them back to COBOL... In the 
process I acquired a language that is really quite useful, especially for 
scripting.

I guess there is an argument that says that all experience is useful in some 
(not always immediately obvious) way. I managed to solve a problem of using 
drag 'n drop in Fujitsu COBOL and could not have done so if I didn't know 
VB...

As my C# confidence increases I am becoming aware of techniques and 
approaches I would never have encountered in COBOL.

All of us should continually strive to expand our skill sets if we are 
serious about computer programming, never mind having more skills to sell in 
the marketplace.

> "I only use COBOL... and I try to do everything."
> copying Pete's example...

LOL! Imitation is the sincerest form of flattery... :-)

Pete
```

#### ↳ Re: .XLS, not .CSV from NETX?

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2009-03-24T21:15:52-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Klhyl.47581$3S3.3586@newsfe22.iad>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<49c923a1$0$5503$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`
- **References:** `<49c923a1$0$5503$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> I create .CSV files in my Net Express programs, then my users can open them 
> with Excel.  But the column widths, bold, line sizes etc. are not set.  I'd 
> like to create a .XLS file that has these characteristics built in.  Is this 
> possible?  I only know COBOL, not C or VB or etc.  TIA, Paul 
> 
Paul,

Softly, softly, catchee monkee :-). There's no easy way when tackling 
something new - you'll have to plug away through the documentation 
available from MicroFocus, (and of course MS).

Some questions first :

(1) Is it just ONE file that you want transferred to Excel or several 
with different record formats
(2) Given Excel Spreadsheet 'A' - presumably your users will want to 
keep different sheets per month, year etc., or is it cumulative for the 
year.
(3) If separate per month, then you need an EMPTY Master Spreadsheet 'A' 
that you can carbon-copy for each month etc., and within OLE programming 
save under different filenames.

Think on it - if I have an MS Access Table for which I want to transfer 
data TO a COBOL file, I could write a CSV from MS Access to update the 
COBOL file or even update the COBOL file directly. Ahh... but if I 
haven't got the FD for the COBOL file, then I'm SOL. Reverse applies - 
before writing from a COBOL file to MS Excel (or Access) I've got to 
know the Spreadsheet/Table format !

Specify your Excel format first giving you a blank Spreadsheet 'A'.  The 
fonts in Excel - that's something you can specify when setting up blank 
spreadsheets - it can of course be done through OLE programming.

Some sources you should be looking at :-

1) MicroFocus Forum - 
http://www.cobolportal.com/microfocusforum/index.asp?bhcp=1

Join and going to Net Express search (in the toolbar) for "XLS", "EXCEL" 
"OLE" etc.

While in the Forum do a search on 'Young Kim' by his name - the message 
is "Calling MS SQL Server APIs", dated 2002 Oct 23 - His code covers ADO 
and uses COBOL files to update EXCEL and ACCESS. If you like what you 
see you could try and contact him for the missing copyfiles from his 
source - Young Kim ysk@kimsoft.com. (He was teaching OO COBOL at a 
university in Ohio).

2) MicroFocus KnowledgeBase (new format) -
http://kb.microfocus.com/display/1n/searchDirect/?searchString=21852**
&searchType=allwords&searchby=keywords&r=0.474005520343781

(I've split the above URL by the two * (asterisks) before 
'&searchType......')

 From the Treeview - go to Net Express

3) M/F On-Line examples -
http://supportline.microfocus.com/examplesandutilities/nesamp.asp

Look at 'SQL' - I know it's SQL but the difference between accessing 
Excel or Accesss is not that great

4) Last and certainly not least - On your own hard disk go to N/E under 
Program Files and from 'Merant' sub-folder to ---> Net Express----> 
Base---->Demos----->OLEDEMOS......> several sub-folders, including Excel

Can't recall which of the above uses it but there's a copyfile 
OLETypeLibrary.cpy. This is an extract of methods applicable for Excel 
taken from the Tool, Type Library Assistant in the IDE.

I think above should keep you busy for a while :-)

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: .XLS, not .CSV from NETX?

## Thread: XP good until 2014 according to Microsoft — 55 this month / 95 total

### XP good until 2014 according to Microsoft

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-03-25T11:56:29+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<72t6ovFrv73bU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I just downloaded and installed IE8.

It went very well; MS installs are much better than they used to be...

Anyway, in the course of exploring new features (and some of them are 
fantastic...I REALLY like the new accellerators that let you look things up 
without leaving the page you are on... you can select a postal address on a 
web page, for example, click the accellerator and have a map with 
instructions on how to get there open in another tabbed window, or select a 
word on a page, click on a different accellerator and have the word defined 
by an online dictionary, all without leaving the page you are on and all 
pretty much instantly... very impressive). I came across this stuff about 
XP. As I know many people here are using it (self included), I thought it 
might be of interest...

http://www.microsoft.com/windows/windows-xp/future.aspx

Obviously, MS are pretty keen to move us all  (kicking and screaming) onto 
Vista, but this page attempts to put their point of view and I thought it 
seems quite fair.

Pete.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz wrote:
> On Mar 26, 12:46 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> it can be read. Why do they do that ?

Because Cascading Style Sheets allow web sites to be developed with a 
consistent, easily changeable style.

The alternative is to apply styles or formatting to each individual control. 
That means hundreds of attributes have to be changed if you decide to use a 
different font or colour or a few other things. For the same reasons you put 
COBOL record definitions into a COPY book, you put Web attributes into a 
CSS.

When I found that certain things didn't render correctly in Firefox and IE, 
it was a simple matter to amend a style sheet so it was acceptable to both 
Browsers. No checking for versions or features required; just a single 
standard style that both of them can render. If I HADN'T used CSS it would 
have been a nightmare.

As for garish colour systems, sure, some people have wildly varying tastes 
and they consider something to be beautiful, which maybe isn't... bearing in 
mind that "beauty" is a very subjective thing.

(If you really hate someone's colour scheme, why not tell the Web Master? 
Most of us who are developng Web Sites are very interested ot hear feedback, 
both good and bad. I'd still be producing incompatible Web Sites if I hadn't 
been made aware it was a serious issue...

Turning off the style sheet may cause other unexpected effects besides loss 
of colour... If you are not viewing a page in the way which the author 
intended you to view it, you can hardly bitch about it not being correct or 
looking ugly...


>
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> underlying OS render engine, which you now have as IE8.

"All ya gotta do is..." huh?

It took some very talented Chinese people several months to write. I believe 
your simplified dismissal is unwarranted.

> one IE.

I find one version is adequate.

>When Mozilla was in
> limbo MS dis no development at all and IE 6 was all you got.

>In fact

I never ever felt "stuck" with IE 6. I found it did what I wanted at the

That looked very reasonable to me. It is interesting that despite all the MS 
hate around, people would still rather pirate Windows than use a "free" OS.

Business has always been competitive. It is normal, natural, and healthy for 
large businesses to keep an eye on their competitors.

I see nothing nefarious in that.

As long as there is competition we all win. (And even if there isn't, as 
long as we get what we want, we still win...)

>
>>> When Mozilla was in
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Firefox took some share and MS reacted with IE7 and now IE8.

Rank Xerox had a monopoly for over 20 years and everyone just used their 
copiers and was gald to have that facility. When their copyright expired and 
companies like UBIX and Canon announced machines with more features, they 
then had to improve their products. Today, we expect to have photcopy 
capability incorporated into our printers, and we expect to have it all for 
a reasonable price. The average person doesn't have a Xerox photocopier 
because they concentrated on the corporate market and bulk volume 
photocopying.

There is nothing "wrong" with a company providing a product that people are 
happy with, and NOT improving it until they have to.

I deliver software that meets customer needs. If they suggest improvements, 
I listen and build a new release. If they are happy with what they have I 
don't go "looking for trouble".

I don't know any company that would. (Obviously, if the competition has 
something extra, that would also be cause to revisit the current solution).

Yet you are so blinded by hatred for Microsoft that when THEY do this, it is 
somehow reprehensible in your eyes. You appear to see it as the wicked 
Goliath crushing the poor noble little David. Do you honestly think that if 
the places of Jobs and Gates (for example) had been reversed, the world 
would be any different?

Did you ever consider how Microsoft became so successful in the first place? 
They provided something that was what people needed, in a form that made it 
easy to assimilate. Sure, they may have used other people's ideas, but they 
bought and paid for them. The result was that the general consumer didn't 
have to go to several different shops to acquire stuff, and didn't have to 
search for emerging techologies in companies no-one had heard of. It was 
just easy to go Microsoft and the world did. I don't think even Bill Gates 
had any idea how successful their company was going to be in say, 1982.

Like all phenomenally successful enterprises, there will be those who resent 
their success. IBM experienced the same tall poppy syndrome right throughout 
the 60s and 70s. (I wouldn't mind betting you felt the same way then about 
IBM, as you do now about Microsoft, and it is no surprise to me that you 
chose to work on ICL mainframes rather than IBM ones.) I was one who was 
appalled by their perceived misuse of power. Ironically, I ended up working 
for them and it changed my perception. There WERE IBM people who did some 
quite dreadful things in pursuit of achieving targets, but they were a 
minority, and they were NOT the company. It is the same with Microsoft.

Despite all the personal abuse and contumely, Bill Gates has not stopped his 
personal philanthropy, has placed his stamp on Microsoft, and millions of 
people throughout the world have benefitted. The company has been organised 
to ensure that a slice of the action is redistributed to the communities. 
Some of that is promoting the company, but it is no less valuable for that.

You have a position supporting free and open software, (I am sympathetic) 
yet when MS give away very usable versions of products like C#, C++, VB and 
Visual Studio, with no strings attached, you don't applaud it.

(For myself, I believe that picking up these products and learning to use 
them is the best thing I have ever done in my whole IT career. It has 
expanded my horizons, enabled me to do things I couldn't do before, and it 
is really fun.I appreciate that Microsoft made it possible, but I also use 
other products from other vendors.)

I simply don't understand how you can be so passionately anti-MS (although I 
respect your right to be so...:-))

…[26 more quoted lines elided]…

On Mar 27, 1:26 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> >http://www.neowin.net/news/main/09/02/25/linux-is-more-of-a-threat-th...
>
> That looked very reasonable to me. It is interesting that despite all the MS
> hate around, people would still rather pirate Windows than use a "free" OS.

One of the consequences of OEMs bundling Windows with (almost) every
machine is that Windows is seen as being "free" with every machine,
when in fact the cost is part of the price. There is often no
comparison of 'PC with Windows' vs 'PC without Windows'.

Because of this perception many, especially third world, think that
there is no difference between getting it "free" with a machine, and
getting it "free" without a new machine.

Also MS have commented that they prefer, and encourage, piracy rather
than have some other system on machines.

In many cases the users do not know that the PC they bought has a
pirated version of Windows on it. It is the dealers installing it and
keeping the cost of Windows.

> Business has always been competitive. It is normal, natural, and healthy for
> large businesses to keep an eye on their competitors.

It is not an 'eye' it is their foot on the throat, not only of their
competitors but also their 'partners'.

For example when MS wanted a browser they got Spyglass to write IE
based on Mosaic. The deal was that MS would pay Spyglass $5 for every
copy _sold_. MS decided to give it away thus not paying their
'partner' and making them go out of business.

They then paid OEMs $5 by way of a discount to _not_ install Mozilla.

> I see nothing nefarious in that.

fanbois often do not.

> As long as there is competition we all win. (And even if there isn't, as
> long as we get what we want, we still win...)
>

Generally when there is no competition the price increases, the
monopoly profiteers.

…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> then had to improve their products.

And reduce their prices.

> Today, we expect to have photcopy
> capability incorporated into our printers, and we expect to have it all for
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> happy with, and NOT improving it until they have to.

Were you 'happy' to pay several thousand dollars for one ?


> I deliver software that meets customer needs. If they suggest improvements,
> I listen and build a new release. If they are happy with what they have I
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> something extra, that would also be cause to revisit the current solution).

That sounds like 'normal competition' but MS does not do that. When DR-
DOS 5 had a far superior product to MS-DOS 4.01 they did not make a
better MS-DOS (not for nearly two years), they built the AARD code
into Windows as FUD and then bundled MS-DOS and Windows at the Windows
price to OEMs to eliminate DR-DOS (which by then had a 15% market
share because it was much better).


> Yet you are so blinded by hatred for Microsoft that when THEY do this, it is
> somehow reprehensible in your eyes. You appear to see it as the wicked
> Goliath crushing the poor noble little David.

Actually many 'Davids' have been crushed by MS.

> Do you honestly think that if
> the places of Jobs and Gates (for example) had been reversed, the world
> would be any different?

Of course it would, by at least those two.

> Did you ever consider how Microsoft became so successful in the first place?
> They provided something that was what people needed, in a form that made it
> easy to assimilate. Sure, they may have used other people's ideas,

No, they used other peoples _products_.

The original SCP-DOS (QDOS) was based on CP/M code translated to 8086
by an Intel code converter. This was demonstrably so when MS-DOS 1.0
has an obscure bug that occurred in CP/M 1.4. Both SCP and MS were DRI
OEMs and had every piece of OEM development software that DRI
distributed to OEMs.

When PC-DOS was announced DRI was able to show it contained an
encrypted DRI copyright. IBM settled by rewriting much of it, and
giving DRI rights to clone it. This is why DR-DOS, with FAT support,
was never subject to litigation from MS. IBM also agreed to sell CP/
M-86 for the IBM-PC but never updated it beyond 1.0.

> but they
> bought and paid for them.

Not always true. see Doublespace.

"""MS-DOS shipped with disk compression software, first "Doublespace",
which Microsoft stole source code from Stac Electronics' "Stacker"
for, they got sued and Stac won, so then they wrote their own code,
and called it "Drivespace"."""

A number of the add-ons in MS-DOS 5 and 6 were not paid for. Central
Point software was licenced, at no cost to MS on the basis that there
would get upgrade revenues.


> The result was that the general consumer didn't
> have to go to several different shops to acquire stuff, and didn't have to
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> their success.

No, I don't resent their success, only the methods that they used to
get there, and their imperialism.

I worked for ICL, of course I used their machines. Duh.

> I was one who was
> appalled by their perceived misuse of power. Ironically, I ended up working
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Actually the misue of power was exactly why both companies, the whole
company, were convicted by the anti-trust court cases.

The misuse of power was directed from the top, as found by evidence
presented to the courts.


> Despite all the personal abuse and contumely, Bill Gates has not stopped his

While the foundation may be giving away amounts of medicines this is
tied to governments pledging not to purchase alternate non-patent
suppliers. Bill has massive share holding in the companies involved
and makes money from his 'philanthropy'.


> You have a position supporting free and open software, (I am sympathetic)

What makes you think it has 'no strings attached' ? It only runs on

it is often free as in beer (and sometimes not) the main point is that
it is 'free' as in speech.

But why should I applaud it ? Why can't I remain completely
indifferent to C# and Visual Studio ?

IE was given away to a) not have to pay Spyglass for the work they
did; b) drive Mozilla* out of business**.

* Mozilla had announced that the next generation of software would be
browser based (ie like cloud computing) and that the fat desktop OS
would be obsolete. MS drove Mozilla out of business sufficiently to
stop development putting computing back 10 years.

** while Mozilla could be had for free for personal use the corporates
did have to buy it.


> (For myself, I believe that picking up these products and learning to use

Yes, you (and Microsoft) were rather late coming to modern tools. It
is natural that you would attach to these, it is called the 'baby duck

http://www.ericsink.com/Browser_Wars.html

"""Internet Explorer 2.0 was basically Spyglass Mosaic with not too
many changes.  IE 3.0 was a major upgrade, but still largely based on
our code.  IE 4.0 was closer to a rewrite, but our code was still
lingering around -- we could tell by the presence of certain esoteric
bugs that were specific to our layout engine."""

http://www.experiencefestival.com/a/History_of_Internet_Explorer_-_Early_beginnings_Spyglass_Mosaic/id/5130160

"""Internet Explorer is derived from Spyglass Mosaic. Originally,
Spyglass licensed the technology and trademarks from NCSA for
producing their own web browser but never used any of the NCSA Mosaic
source code [1]. In 1995 Spyglass Mosaic was licensed by Microsoft, in
an arrangement under which Spyglass would receive a quarterly fee plus
a percentage of Microsoft's revenues for the software."""

So, while Spyglass did not use Mosaic for their own browser, Microsoft
did.

Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> Interesting reading.
…[94 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Pete, Microsoft is it's own biggest competitor. If it did NOT improve its 
software and sell newer versions, its revenue stream would wither 
considerably.

As with most monopolies and near-monopolies, the consumer is the big 
beneficiary. The versions in use today - of Windows or Office - are worlds 
superior to the equivalent products of 15 years ago. And they cost about the 
same.

You're correct that Microsoft bought a lot of technology and the opponents 
of Microsoft are quick to point that out. There is one item that Microsoft 
DID develop on its own and that one item changed the face of computing: 
Microsoft Office and its ability to access data seamlessly between 
applications.

I recall a guy testifying before Congress on Microsoft's "monopoly" 
stranglehold. His company developed some high-end application and he 
testified that Windows was a godsend. He said his company had a room as big 
as an airplane hanger (probably an exaggeration) full of printers. Every 
time his company had a new release, they had to test it with scores of 
printers and every time a new printer hit the market, his developers had to 
devote untold resources to making his software work with the new device. 
Windows made all that silliness go away and his programmers could 
concentrate on the application itself.

I know in our bread-and-butter product under DOS, the things that gave us 
the most fits were printers and modems. No longer.

As to those afflicted with MDS (Microsoft Derangement Syndrome), they are 
similar to their counterparts cursed with Bush Derangement Syndrome. (I 
wouldn't be surprised if there was a great deal of overlap inasmuch as Eric 
Hoffer discovered that membership in mass movements is interchangeable.)

They went to small liberal arts colleges (big is bad) and are almost always 
Communists (the collective is good). For these people, they can endure 
without a god, but there must be a devil somewhere. They cannot survive 
without something to despise.

HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[99 more quoted lines elided]…
> considerably.

Yes, I can see that. I think Richard's position is that they do this from a 
desire to strangle the competition, rather than any desire to improve for 
the sake of it. The question here is would they continue investment in 
improving products if there was no competition?

I believe it doesn't matter, as long as people are happy with the products. 
I'm not suggesting we shouldn't strive to improve anything, whether driven 
by the competition or customer complaints and request for enhancement, or 
not. There is great value in the search for excellence. But in a commercial 
environment that search is usually driven by the marketplace, rather than a 
desire for personal growth :-) (The personal growth occurs as a by-product 
of the struggle initiatiated by competing in the marketplace...)
>
> As with most monopolies and near-monopolies, the consumer is the big
> beneficiary. The versions in use today - of Windows or Office - are
> worlds superior to the equivalent products of 15 years ago. And they
> cost about the same.

Absolutely. Many people are realising that things have improved.

>
> You're correct that Microsoft bought a lot of technology and the
> opponents of Microsoft are quick to point that out.

I don't see why that should be considered "bad".  Many corporations improve 
their products and services through mergers and acquisitions and it is 
considered standard business practice. If you buy a competitior so you can 
"close them down" that is simply part of a business strategy. Small 
competitors should protect themselves from hostile takeovers (ensure 
shareholders are properly informed and engaged, or retain private ownership 
until the company has the resources to resist hostile takeover), or ensure 
that if they are taken over, everybody gets enough out of it to be smiling 
all the way to the bank.


> There is one item
> that Microsoft DID develop on its own and that one item changed the
> face of computing: Microsoft Office and its ability to access data
> seamlessly between applications.

I wholeheartedly agree. Online Linking and Embedding (which evolved into the 
Component Object Model (COM)) has completely changed the way in which 
systems can be built. I have a few things to say about this and they are on 
the new Cobdata Web site which should be launched in the next few days. 
After decades building mainframe systems with the Waterfall (SDLC), I am 
completely convinced that an Agile style of development using iteration and 
interaction, with user involvement throughout is just many times better. It 
is like Cro-Magnon replacing Neanderthal. The new ways are smarter, faster, 
more resilient, and more efficient. The single most enabling technology 
behind it is the Component Object Model.
>
> I recall a guy testifying before Congress on Microsoft's "monopoly"
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> movements is interchangeable.)

I see no connection. :-)

>
> They went to small liberal arts colleges (big is bad) and are almost
> always Communists (the collective is good).

I believe collectives can be good, but I'm not a Communist.

(I remember as a teenager listening to a Communist explaining Marxist 
principles, on a Summer afternoon in Albert Park in Auckland. "To each, 
according to this needs, from each according to his ability."  I remember 
being astonished at the beauty of such a simple and noble idea. As a capable 
Human Being I have always felt some tendency to accept responsibility and 
help those less able. The problem with the statement is in the word "need". 
I remember coming to the conclusion that it could never work, despite the 
assurance that it WAS working in Russia.

Many years later I had cause to visit Eastern Europe (East Germany and 
CzechoSlovakia (as it was then)), still under the Russian yoke and saw at 
first hand how well it was "working". Anyone persuaded to Communism only 
needed to spend 30 minutes driving across the border from an affluent West 
Germany into CzechoSlovakia, where there were still bombed out villages from 
WWII (36 years after the event), where people with sad faces in tattered 
clothing, comforted hungry children, and listless eyes avoided contact. I 
was in an open sports car and it is the only time in my life I have felt 
sorry to be affluent. While our Democracies are far from perfect, there is 
NO comparison between them and Communism as an alternative. No wonder the 
world voted with its feet.

> For these people, they
> can endure without a god, but there must be a devil somewhere. They
> cannot survive without something to despise.

That is one of the planks in my platform of atheism. If I believed in God, 
I'd have to believe in the Devil. As I believe that "Good" and "Bad" are 
very Human inventions (the Universe doesn't care; events happen in 
accordance with random probabilities and there is no consideration as to 
whether they SHOULD happen for moral or ethical reasons, any more than the 
Universe considers them Good or Bad), believing in the ultimate 
personification of either Good or Bad just seems illogical to me.

(Please don't tell me I can't be ethical or moral unless I believe in God. 
It is a tired argument and I find it offensive. If Human Beings are to live 
together in communities and groups as we are increasingly forced to, it 
simply makes sense to behave well. Life is easier for everyone if we all 
behave decently. It has nothing to do with Judgement or threats of Hellfire, 
it is just simple common sense.)

Pete.

Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
>> For these people, they
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> seems illogical to me.

Hoffer didn't mean to get off on religion - he was saying that a mass 
movement need not have an affirmative goal so much as it must have something 
to hate. The "Save the Whales" movement would wither without whalers! If 
whales were dying from chastic fibrosis (a disease usually found in 
badgers), a few people might care, but there would be no mass movement.

Your atheism plank is slightly flawed. There is no "devil" in Judaism, for 
example. There is no Hell. There IS a "Satan," but he's a good guy.* "Evil" 
is simply defined as the absense of good. You can turn off the light, but 
you can't buy a "dark bulb." The Jewish view is that God created everything 
and God doesn't make junk.

>
> (Please don't tell me I can't be ethical or moral unless I believe in
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Judgement or threats of Hellfire, it is just simple common sense.)

Again, Judaism (for example) has no belief test. The official view is that 
God wants righteous conduct. As such, one can be a righteous Jew, a 
righteous Baptist, a righteous Buddhist, or a righteous atheist. To the 
degree that any system teaches and expects justice, charity, lovingkindness, 
compassion, and so forth, that system is a righteous system.

--------
* "SATAN" is mentioned only four times in the Hebrew Scriptures. In each 
case, he is defined as an agent of God. In the Christian tradition, most 
occurrences of a wicked figure are interpreted as Satan by another name. For 
example, the Christian belief holds that the serpent in the Garden of Eden 
was really Satan in a snake-suit; the Jewish teaching is that the serpent 
was, well, a serpent.

Cites, please?  I am familiar with the Book of Job, a variation of the 
god-as-Trickster tale, and from what I can tell 'satan' (lit., 
'adversary') is always prefaced there with a definite article 
('ha-satan', 'the adversary').  There's no mention of this adversary 
anywhere I can find in the Pentateuch and other mentions are much, *much*

vision).

sons of the god(s) (lit. 'ha-Elohim') and also came the adversary (lit. 
'ha-satan') amongst/amiddle/in their midst'; notice how the adversary is 
given a special notice, as though to say 'the sons of god' did not include 
him... furthered by the questioning in Job 1:7, where an (omniscient?) 
deity asks the adversary about comings and goings)

>DD

Christians believe that Satan is fallen angel; that is rebelled
against God and was thrown out of heaven by God; he is out to undo
everything God has done and to capture his own souls.

Jew do not believe this at all.  

The Hebrew word "Satan" means "Hinderer." To hinder someone means to
hold him back, to try to prevent him from doing something. God created
the Hinderer to give us work to do in this world according to Judaism.
Satan is here to make things difficult for us, so we can overcome our
evil temptations, and pass the test. That is the purpose of Satan.
Satan is an angel whose purpose has been determined by God.

Temptation, according to Judaism,  is there to try and deter us. It
gives us the ability to do the wrong thing. More importantly, it gives
us the ability to look at evil and refuse to do it. By presenting us
with the opportunity to do evil, it gives us the ability to choose
between good and evil.

I guess, in a sense, the purpose of Satan is the same in both
religions.  Satan just has a different persona in each.  

More can be learned about the Jewish Satan in the Jewish Encyclopedia:

http://www.jewishencyclopedia.com/view.jsp?letter=S&artid=270

And to say Judaism doesn't have hell is just not true.  They don't
have a hell like Christians have, but they do indeed have a hell.

This article best describes Judaism's beliefs about the afterlife:

http://judaism.about.com/library/3_askrabbi_o/bl_simmons_heavenhell.htm

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> Your atheism plank is slightly flawed.

Gee Jerry, d'ya think...? :-)

Guess I better change my mind...:-)

(For some reason, I find an authoritative statement against it, just 
entrenches my position. My mind has never been changed by people saying 
ITSA; it has (surprisingly often) been changed by people making reasoned 
arguments.)


>There is no "devil" in
> Judaism, for example. There is no Hell. There IS a "Satan," but he's
> a good guy.* "Evil" is simply defined as the absense of good. You can
> turn off the light, but you can't buy a "dark bulb."

Just as "cold" is the absence of "heat". Yes, I understand that. However, if 
you personify the ultimate good, then the absolute absence of good can also 
be personified, and should be, to balance the dichotomy. Unless, of course, 
God is NOT considered to be the personification of ultimate good.

Most religions do not take that view, seeing God as being the 
personification of all that is good, and therefore requiring the balancing 
effect of the "adversary".

Dichotomy as the basis for religion was around  for several thousand years 
before both Judaism and Christianity. It may have been the basis for 
polytheism, as each dichotomy could have its own "god" and "adversary" 
representing the theoretical ends of the dichotomy, which were unattainable 
in real life. The Sanskrit Vedas (amongst the earliest written records on 
the planet - ca 7500 BCE) use this view. It is likely that other religions 
derived from it directly or indirecty, or possibly reached the same 
conclusion independently.

This leads to the interesting dilemma of impossibility. It goes like this:

1. God is all powerful and all good.
2. The "Enemy" is not quite as powerful because he was created by God, but 
he is totally Evil.
3. God struggles with the Enemy and will ultimately overcome him.

Immediate questions:

1. How and why would a being who is "all good" create something that is pure 
Evil?
2. Why is the struggle taking so long? If God is all poweful, why hasn't the 
Evil one been vanquished long ago?
3. If God permits Evil in the world because it is "good for us" how can He 
be all good?

It is the same dilemma as:

Could God create a rock so huge and heavy that even He couldn't move it?



>The Jewish view
> is that God created everything and God doesn't make junk.
>
And yet there is junk in the world.

Either God DIDN'T make EVERYTHING or God DOES make junk.

( Hear now, the World according to Pete: In actual fact, there is no God and 
the Universe is amoral. The atoms comprising the junk are no different from 
the atoms comprising a similar work of art. It is only "junk" because 
Humanity considers it so. Despite our arrogant protestations to the 
contrary, and belief that the Universe is all about us, we are, in fact, a 
side effect. The Universe is a star factory. The fact that we are here is 
because in an almost infinite series of probabilities (the Universe is 
boundless, not infinite), the most unlikely events will happen. The 
evolution of life on this planet is just such an unlikely event. (SETI have 
been searchng for 30 years and have now covered everything within 30 light 
years... no discernable intelligent life. Doesn't mean there isn't any, but 
it is certainly an unusual and unlikely event.) There's nothing wrong with 
there being no God. Life goes on as it must. We think and act and work and 
have fun and do the best we can, not to please our Imaginary Friend, or to 
buy a place in the next life - sorry, bad news on that front too; might as 
well make the best of the life you have...), but because it makes sense to 
do so. Accept responsibility, have compassion for your fellow Man; the world 
will be a better place. Don't blame God. It isn't His fault... we invented 
Him. Get over it.  Here endeth the lesson...:-))
>>
>> (Please don't tell me I can't be ethical or moral unless I believe in
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> righteous system.
For me it doesn't matter whether God wants it or not. Your "righteous 
conduct" is required by common sense. (As long as we live in groups...if you 
accept the life of a hermit or recluse, and don't interact with others, you 
can be as wicked and mean as you like. Beat yourself up all day...)

> --------
> * "SATAN" is mentioned only four times in the Hebrew Scriptures. In
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> the Jewish teaching is that the serpent was, well, a serpent.

I was going to launch into a scathing dismissal of the Pentateuch but I 
realised there is no point in offending people who actually believe this as 
literal truth, and no minds are going to be changed.

Let's just say I don't accept it as literal truth so I don't care whether it 
was Evil in a snake suit or just a snake. It is like I should worry whether

Mr Dashwood, a bit of Simple Logic would banish such a worry and close 
reading of the definitive text might lead to the ready conclusion that it 
was 'neither of the above'.  According to Brother Grimm's relating of the 
tale 'Kleine Schneewittchen', as translated by D L Ashliman ( 
http://www.pitt.edu/~dash/grimm053.html ) Snow White's apple was poisoned 
in such a manner that a piece of it lodged in her throat.

These being pre-Heimlich-manuever days the dwarves placed her in a glass 
coffin; the Prince begs the dwarves to release the coffin to him and one 
of the Prince's servants stumbles while carrying it, dislodging the 
apple-chunk and allowing for her reviving.

Obviously this all happened in the Oldene Dayse, when Princes were Good 
and Lassies were chaste... so it would not have been until after their 
wedding and appropriately sanctified uninion that the Prince would have 
given her...

... arse-a-nick.

(At my Alma Mater there was a tradition of the Friday Night Lecture, when

curriculum.  There is a tale - perhaps apocryphal - of a professor talking

period - note it was not called 'Question and Answer', as some Questions 
might be found which have no Answer - one of the students raised a hand, 
was recognised by the speaker, stood and said (this student, as many 
others, having read the dialogue (or portions thereof) in the original 
Ancient Greek 'Mr Jones... it is Meno*n*.')

On Tue, 31 Mar 2009 14:11:50 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>(For some reason, I find an authoritative statement against it, just 
>entrenches my position. My mind has never been changed by people saying 
>ITSA; it has (surprisingly often) been changed by people making reasoned

That works as long as our basic values aren't what people want to
change.

Logic and reason don't change basic values - about the only thing that
does is a conflict with values that are even more basic.

For instance, a belief in a God that allows eternal torture of all
people who don't believe in the right way may conflict with "my
daughter is a good person, and I love her".

But the simple logic of "If everything requires a creator - who
created God" has a chance of eliminating that first argument - but not
the basic belief.

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

One implicit assumption in this is the one that "life like us" is the
proper goal (or at least partial goal) of creation.   In particular,
our culture that includes radio and other technology is the way things
should be.    We rate our type of intelligence and technology highly,
because those characteristics define ourselves.    We assume that life
has to move towards the apex which is ourselves.

The universe is unimaginably huge.   Whether or not there is something
similar to what we call "life" or "intelligence" is something we have
no data points to extrapolate from.   (or one data point, which is
insufficient)   It may be that we never will find out.

It seems odd to find Creationists who believe that the Creator would
only create life on one small planet, all based around one particular
species (or at least those members of that species who believe as they
do).

There's the rub.  On the one hand, without generalisations, the Universe 
becomes atomised, a series of discrete events which may or may not have 
any sort of connection (generalisation) between them.

On the other hand... a heavier object is harder to pick up, therefore it 
is more likely to fall faster than a lighter one, no?  Leaving Galilleo 
aside... a parachute which fails to open would fall as fast as one which 
opens.

Strikes me that that's just a lazy person's way of not countering the
argument.  Mathematical generalizations are always correct; less abstract
statements can be just as valid.  If I say, for instance, that "rain usually
does not come from a clear sky" I doubt if anyone would bother to call me on
it.

> >I was taught it as 'generalisations are, quite often, useless... this one
> >included'.

Self-contradicting statements are meaningless.  (This one is not
self-contradicting, due to the qualification).

> Quite often.   But I suspect they are useful more often than not.   We
> think using patterns and generalizations allowing us to extrapolate
> from our past experiences.   Sometimes we guess wrongly, but more
> often it works.
>

I'd say that it's possible to compose any real-world generalization such
that it's correct.  Generalizations are useful in establishing broad
principles.  They aren't automatically wrong.
